The Objection and Refutation by Impossibility
Objection to existentiality and refutation by naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection that necessity and possibility are existential because they are truly predicated, and the refutation by showing that impossibility and non-existence are also truly predicated but not existent.

Core Idea
True predication does not guarantee existence, as shown by impossibility and non-existence.

The author begins by considering the claim that attributes like necessity (wujub) and possibility (imkan) must be existential because they are truly predicated. To counter this, he presents a refutation (naqd) using impossibility (imtina') and non-existence ('adam)—attributes that are truly predicated yet universally acknowledged as non-existent. This lesson introduces that objection and the initial answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated: that if necessity and possibility are truly predicated, they must be existential.
The objection is implied in the phrase 'فيكون كل منهما وجوديا'.
2
Find the refutation
Look for the answer starting with 'والجواب: النقض بالامتناع والعدم'. This is a counter-example: impossibility and non-existence are also truly predicated but not existent.
The refutation uses two attributes that are clearly non-existent.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how this naqd refutes the original claim. It shows that true predication does not entail existence, so necessity and possibility could be non-existential too.
This is a common kalam argument form: producing a counter-example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثاني : أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها في الخارج

ذلك ) في وجوب الواجب ( ولم يخرج ) به ( الواجب عن كونه واجباً)، وهكذا الحال في الإمكان، فيكون كل منهما وجوديا . ( والجواب : النقض بالامتناع والعدم إذ كل منهما ثابت لموصوفه سواء وجد فرض من عقل أم لم ،يوجد، وليس شيء منهما موجوداً بالضرورة والاتفاق والحل : أن يقال اتصاف الذات بصفة في الخارج أو نفس الأمر لا يقتضي كون تلك الصفة موجودة في أحدهما، ألا يرى أن زيداً أعمى في الخارج وليس العمي موجوداً فيه، وذلك لأن الموجود في الخارج ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده لا ظرفاً لاتصاف شيء آخر به. وكذا الحال في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزم من كون الصفة كالوجوب والإمكان مثلاً أمراً عدمياً اعتبارياً أن لا يكون شيء موصوا موصوفاً بها
بحيث يشمل الواجب تعالى أيضاً فلا نسلم الملازمة لأنه إذا انتفى الواجب لم يكن متصفاً بالوجوب، ولأن انتفاءها محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال .

قوله : ( والحل إلخ) منع اللزوم المستفاد من قوله : فيلزم أن لا يكون الواجب واجباً إلخ لما أن انتفاء مبدأ المحمول في الخارج أو في نفس الأمر لا يستلزم انتفاء صحة الحمل والاتصاف، لتحقق الاتصاف بالصفات العدمية وحملها على موصوفاتها، نعم إنه فرع تحقق الموصوف في
ظرف الاتصاف .

قوله: (موصوفاً بها اتصافاً انتزاعياً بمعنى أنه في نفسه بحيث إذا لاحظه العقل بالقياس إلى الوجود انتزع عنه الوجوب ووصفه به فاندفع ما قيل : إن اتصافه بالوجوب ليس في الخارج وإلا لزم تقدم وجوده على وجوبه فهو عقلي، فإذا فرض انتفاء العقول يلزم أن لا يكون الواجب واجباً لانتفاء ظرف الاتصاف لا لأن الاتصاف فرع تحقق الوجوب، حتى يتم الجواب المذكور واندفع أيضاً ما قيل : إنه حينئذ يشكل قولهم ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له إذ لا ثبوت
عدم ما سوى الواجب تعالى من المبادئ العالية وغيرها وليس يجوز تحقق وجوب الواجب حينئذ باعتبار فرض نفس ،موصوفه لأنه يتوقف على وجوده المسبوق بالوجوب، فلو توقف وجوبه على فرضه ،دار فتأمل.

قوله : (لا تقتضي كون إلخ فيه بحث لأن اتصاف الشيء بالشيء يستلزم نسبة لا يتصور تحققها إلا بين شيئين متمايزين ولا تمايز إلا مع ثبوت كل من المتمايزين في الجملة، فلا يتصور ثبوت شيء لشيء واتصافه به في نفس الأمر بدون تحقق كل من الصفة والموصوف فيها والحق أن اتصاف أمر في نفس الأمر بصفة معدومة فيها مما لامرية فيه : ألا يرى أنا إذا تصورنا المعدوم مثلاً اتصف بوجوده في الذهن، مع أن وجوده فيه ليس بموجود لا في الخارج وهو ظاهر، ولا في الذهن إذ لم يتصور وجوده قطعاً، لكن قاعدتهم تقتضي انتفاء ذلك الاتصاف وإن صرحوا بخلافه اللهم إلا أن يقال : المتمايزان في الجملة لا يستلزم الثبوت أصلاً، كما سيشير إليه الشارح في بحث العلم من موقف الأعراض فتدبر .
The Third Observatory - The Second Objective: Considerational matters that have no existence in the external world.

That (is) regarding the necessity of the Necessary Being, and it does not remove the Necessary Being from being necessary. And similarly the case in possibility, so each of them is existential. (And the answer: refutation by impossibility and non-existence, for each of them is established for its described thing whether a mental supposition exists or not, and neither of them is existent necessarily and by agreement. And the solution: that it is said the attribution of an essence with an attribute in the external or in the self-same thing does not necessitate that attribute to be existent in either of them. Do you not see that Zayd is blind in the external and blindness is not existent in him? That is because what is existent in the external is that for which the external is a locus of its existence, not a locus of the attribution of something else to it. And likewise the case in the self-same thing. So it does not follow from a matter like necessity and possibility, for example, being a non-existential considerational matter, that nothing is described with it such that it includes the Necessary Being, exalted. So we do not grant the implication because if the Necessary Being were negated, it would not be described with necessity, and because its negation is impossible, so it is possible that it entails the impossible.

His saying: (And the solution, etc.) is a prevention of the implication derived from his saying: then it would follow that the Necessary Being is not necessary, etc. because the non-existence of the foundation of the predicate in the external or in the self-same thing does not necessitate the negation of the validity of predication and attribution, due to the realization of attribution by non-existential attributes and their predication on their described things. Yes, it is a branch of the realization of the described thing in the locus of attribution.

His saying: (described with it by an abstractive attribution) meaning that it is in itself such that when the intellect observes it in relation to existence, it abstracts from it necessity and describes it with it. So what was said is repelled: that its attribution with necessity is not in the external, otherwise its existence would precede its necessity, so it is mental. So if the negation of intellects is supposed, it would follow that the Necessary Being is not necessary due to the negation of the locus of attribution, not because attribution is a branch of the realization of necessity, until the mentioned answer is completed. And also repelled is what was said: that then their statement 'the establishment of something for something is a branch of the establishment of that for which it is established' would be problematic, since there is no establishment of non-existence besides the Necessary Being, exalted, from the high principles and others, and it is not permissible that the necessity of the Necessary Being be realized then by considering the supposition of its described thing itself, because it depends on its existence which is preceded by necessity. So if its necessity depends on its supposition, a vicious circle would occur. So reflect.

His saying: (Does not necessitate that, etc.) There is a discussion in it because the attribution of a thing to a thing necessitates a relation whose realization is inconceivable except between two distinct things, and there is no distinction except with the establishment of each of the two distinct things in some respect. So the establishment of a thing for a thing and its attribution to it in the self-same thing is inconceivable without the realization of each of the attribute and the described thing in it. But the truth is that the attribution of a matter in the self-same thing by a non-existent attribute in it is something without doubt. Do you not see that when we imagine the non-existent, for example, it is described by its existence in the mind, even though its existence in it is not existent—neither in the external, which is apparent, nor in the mind, since its existence was not conceived at all. But their rule necessitates the negation of that attribution, even though they explicitly state otherwise. Unless it is said: the two distinct things in some respect do not necessitate establishment at all, as the commentator will indicate in the discussion of knowledge from the Station of Accidents. So reflect.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument and commentary on whether attributes like necessity (wujub) and possibility (imkan) are existential (wujudiyya) or non-existential/considerational ('adamiyya/i'tibariyya). It begins with the claim that these attributes are existential because they are truly predicated. The objection is raised by citing impossibility (imtina') and non-existence ('adam), which are also truly predicated but are not existent. The answer (jawab) is a refutation (naqd) by pointing out that these are not existent, and the solution (hall) is that attribution in the external or in the self-same thing (nafs al-amr) does not require the attribute to be existent. The example of Zayd being blind is given: blindness is not an existent entity, yet Zayd is described as blind. The commentary explains that the negation of the foundation of the predicate does not negate the validity of predication, because predication can be based on non-existential attributes. It further distinguishes between real attribution and abstractive (intiza'i) attribution. The commentary then addresses objections about the necessity of the Necessary Being and the principle that 'the establishment of something for something is a branch of the establishment of the subject', showing that non-existence of the subject would lead to a circle, but the solution holds. Finally, a discussion about the necessity of distinction between two things for attribution is analyzed, and the truth is asserted that attribution by a non-existent attribute is possible, as in the case of imagining a non-existent thing having mental existence.

The Solution: Attribution Without Existence
Solution to the problem of existentiality and elaboration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the solution that attribution in the external or self-same thing does not require the attribute to exist, and how this preserves the necessity of the Necessary Being without circularity.

Core Idea
Attribution by non-existent attributes is possible, as Zayd is blind without blindness existing.

Having refuted the claim that true predication entails existence, the author now provides a positive solution: an attribute can be truly attributed without being an existent entity. The example of Zayd's blindness illustrates this. This lesson unpacks the solution and addresses further objections about the necessity of the Necessary Being and the principle of distinction between attribute and subject.

Guided Path
1
Understand the solution
Read the passage starting with 'والحل: أن يقال'. The key point is that attribution does not require the attribute to exist in the external or in nafs al-amr.
Example: Zayd is blind in the external world, but blindness is not an existent thing there.
2
Examine the commentary on abstractive attribution
The commentary explains that the attribution is abstractive (intiza'i): the intellect observes the subject and abstracts the attribute. The existence of the attribute is not needed.
This resolves the objection that if the attribute were non-existent, the subject could not be described.
3
Consider the circularity objection and its resolution
The passage addresses a potential circularity: if the necessity of the Necessary Being depends on its supposition, a circle arises. The solution avoids this by showing that the attribution does not depend on the existence of the attribute.
Reflect on how the author's solution breaks the circle.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثاني : أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها في الخارج

ذلك ) في وجوب الواجب ( ولم يخرج ) به ( الواجب عن كونه واجباً)، وهكذا الحال في الإمكان، فيكون كل منهما وجوديا . ( والجواب : النقض بالامتناع والعدم إذ كل منهما ثابت لموصوفه سواء وجد فرض من عقل أم لم ،يوجد، وليس شيء منهما موجوداً بالضرورة والاتفاق والحل : أن يقال اتصاف الذات بصفة في الخارج أو نفس الأمر لا يقتضي كون تلك الصفة موجودة في أحدهما، ألا يرى أن زيداً أعمى في الخارج وليس العمي موجوداً فيه، وذلك لأن الموجود في الخارج ما يكون الخارج ظرفاً لوجوده لا ظرفاً لاتصاف شيء آخر به. وكذا الحال في نفس الأمر، فلا يلزم من كون الصفة كالوجوب والإمكان مثلاً أمراً عدمياً اعتبارياً أن لا يكون شيء موصوا موصوفاً بها

ظرف الاتصاف .

عدم ما سوى الواجب تعالى من المبادئ العالية وغيرها وليس يجوز تحقق وجوب الواجب حينئذ باعتبار فرض نفس ،موصوفه لأنه يتوقف على وجوده المسبوق بالوجوب، فلو توقف وجوبه على فرضه ،دار فتأمل.

The Third Observatory - The Second Objective: Considerational matters that have no existence in the external world.

That (is) regarding the necessity of the Necessary Being, and it does not remove the Necessary Being from being necessary. And similarly the case in possibility, so each of them is existential. Third Argument: Wujub Cannot Be Existential
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument against wujub being an existential attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that necessity (wujub) cannot be an existential attribute because it would lead to circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If necessity were a real attribute, it would need a foundation, causing an infinite regress.

Al-Jurjani presents a sharp logical argument against treating necessity as a real, existential attribute. He shows that whether necessity is identical to the quiddity or additional to it, problems arise. Only if necessity is a non-existential abstraction can these problems be avoided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: the argument proceeds by disjunction.
The passage is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two possibilities
If wujub is existential, it must either be identical to the quiddity or additional to it. Note the consequence of each.
3
Understand the consequences
If identical, then wujub is not additional, contradicting the claim it is an attribute. If additional, then it depends on the quiddity, leading to circularity or regress.
Regress is impossible for the Necessary Being.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since both options are untenable, wujub cannot be existential. It must be non-existential, i.e., a mental abstraction from the essence itself.
This supports the Ash'ari view of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: لو كان الوجوب وجوديا أي موجودا في الخارج لم يكن زائدا على ماهيته بل كان عينها لامتناع الجزئية. وإلا لكان الوجوب الموجود محتاجا إلى الماهية، إذ لا بد أن يكون وجوده مستندا إليها، فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل. بخلاف ما إذا كان عدميا فإنه يجوز أن يكون انتزاعيا محضا من نفس ذاته، فلا احتياج أصلا.
And the third argument: If necessity (al-wujub) were existential, i.e., existing in the external world, it would not be additional to its quiddity (mahiyya) but rather identical to it, because partiality is impossible. Otherwise, if it were not so but were additional to the quiddity, then the existential necessity would need the quiddity, for its existence must be based on it, and then circularity or regress would follow. Unlike the case if it were non-existential, for then it may be a pure abstraction from its very essence, so there is no dependence at all.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that necessity (wujub) is an existential attribute. The argument proceeds disjunctively: either necessity is existential and then either it is identical to the quiddity of the necessary being or it is additional. If it is identical, then it is not extra, which contradicts the assumption that it is an attribute. If it is additional, then this existential necessity depends on the quiddity for its existence, leading to circularity or infinite regress because the necessity itself would require the quiddity to exist, and the quiddity's necessity would require its necessity. Therefore, the only coherent option is that necessity is non-existential, i.e., a purely mental abstraction from the essence itself, involving no real dependence. This argument supports the Ash'ari position that wajib al-wujud is simple and its attributes are not distinct existential entities.

Misconception: One might think necessity must be a real attribute existing in the external world.
Correction: The argument shows that if necessity were real, it would cause an infinite regress or contradiction, so it must be a mental consideration only.

Checkpoint
What follows if wujub is existential and additional to the quiddity?
A) It would be identical to the quiddity.
B) The quiddity would need the necessity.
C) The necessity would need the quiddity, leading to circularity or regress.
D) No problem arises.

Key Takeaway
Necessity (wujub) is not an existential attribute but a mental abstraction, to avoid circularity and regress.

Or do you, deep down, wish He fit neatly into your categories of thought? What does that reveal about your desire for control?

Third Argument: Necessity is Identical to Essence
Third argument: necessity is identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (al-wujub) must be identical to the essence of the Necessary Being, and why treating it as an external accident leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be an attribute added to God's essence; it is the essence itself.

This lesson presents a core argument in Islamic theology: if necessity were an existent attribute distinct from God's essence, it would depend on that essence, making it contingent. The only coherent position is that necessity is identical to the essence. We will walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'لو كان...' (if it were...) introduces the hypothetical.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two options: necessity is either identical to the essence or additional to it. See how the first option is affirmed and the second leads to a contradiction.
The phrase 'وإلا' marks the alternative.
3
Follow the consequence of the second option
If necessity were additional to the essence, it would be an accident needing a substrate, thus contingent. That would require a cause, leading to an infinite regress or circularity, contradicting essential necessity.
The conclusion is that the Necessary would need a cause for its necessity, which is absurd.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The passage concludes that necessity must be identical to the essence. Reflect on why this is the only coherent position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: لو كان الوجوب وجودياً أي موجوداً في الخارج لم يكن زائداً على ماهيته أي ماهية الواجب، بل كان عينها لامتناع الجزئية، وإلا وإن لم يكن كذلك بل كان زائداً على الماهية لكان الوجوب الموجود محتاجاً إلى الماهية، إذ لا بد أن يكون عارضاً لها قائماً بها، والعارض محتاج في وجوده إلى معروضه، فيكون ممكناً مستنداً إلى علة ويعلل بها أي بماهية الواجب لامتناع تعليله بغيرها. ولا احتاج الواجب في وجوبه إلى علة مغايرة لماهيته، فلا يكون واجباً وجوباً ذاتياً.
And third: If necessity (al-wujub) were existent (wujudiyy) i.e., existing in the external, it would not be additional to its essence (mahiyyah) i.e., the essence of the Necessary, but would be identical to it because of the impossibility of part-whole relationship. Otherwise, if it were not so but were additional to the essence, then the existing necessity would be in need of the essence, since it would have to be an accident subsisting in it, and an accident needs its substrate for its existence; so it would be contingent, dependent on a cause, and would be caused by the essence of the Necessary because it cannot be caused by another. Then the Necessary would need a cause for its necessity different from its essence, so it would not be necessary by essential necessity.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (thālithuhā) concerning the nature of necessity (wujūb) in the Necessary Being. It considers two possibilities: either necessity is an existent attribute (wujūdiyy) added to the essence, or it is identical to the essence. The argument assumes that an existent necessity cannot be additional (zā'id) to the essence, because that would imply a part-whole relationship (juz'iyya) which is impossible for the Necessary. If it were additional, it would be an accident (ʿāriḍ) subsisting in the essence, and therefore dependent on the essence for its own existence. That dependence would make it contingent (mumkin), requiring a cause. That cause would be the essence itself, leading to the conclusion that the Necessary would need a cause for its necessity other than itself, contradicting essential necessity (wujūb dhātī). Hence, necessity must be identical to the essence.

وجودي
ماهية
عارض
معروض
ممكن
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that necessity is an attribute added to God's essence, like power or will.
Correction: This argument shows that necessity cannot be additional; it must be identical to the essence, otherwise God would be contingent in some respect. Other attributes (like power) are additional, but necessity is unique.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, if necessity were an accident added to the essence, what would follow?
A) The essence would be simple
B) The necessity would be identical to the essence
C) The necessity would depend on the essence, making it contingent
D) The essence would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Necessity must be identical to God's essence, for only then is He necessary by Himself, not through any cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's necessity, do you truly believe that He depends on nothing—not even your worship, your actions, or your existence? How does this change how you approach your daily needs?
?
If God's necessity is identical to His essence, then nothing outside of Him can affect Him. What does this mean for your fears of people's opinions or worldly outcomes? Are you seeking refuge in the Necessary One or in contingent things?
?
The argument shows that God cannot be caused. Is there any part of your life where you act as if God needs your good deeds or your devotion? How can you shift from a transactional relationship to one of pure dependence on the Necessary Being?

Objection that Wajib is a Relation and Its Refutation
An objection-and-answer unit on the nature of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that necessity (wujub) is a relation does not undermine the identity of wajib and essence, and how the Ash'ari view of relations as mental considerations resolves the challenge.

Core Idea
Relations are mental, not real—so the objection collapses.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if necessity (wujub) is a relation, then the necessary being would be posterior to its essence, implying that necessity is distinct and additional. The author refutes this by clarifying that the identity claim is conditional on existence, and that relations, being mental considerations, have no real existence that would generate the objection. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part (from 'لا يقال' to 'زائداً عليها') which presents the argument that if wujub is a relation, it must be posterior and thus not identical to the essence.
Identify the three premises: wujub is a relation, relations are posterior to relata, so wujub is posterior to the essence.
2
Read the refutation
Read the author's response (from 'لأنا نقول' to the end) and note how he qualifies the identity claim and argues that the objection's premise contradicts the assumption of existence.
Notice the pivot: the identity is not absolute but conditional on existence, and relations are mental, so wujub cannot be a real relation.
3
Analyze the key move
Understand why the Ash'ari view that relations are 'umur i'tibariyya' (mental considerations) is crucial. This undermines the objector's claim that wujub has any real existence that would make it distinct.
The objector assumes relations are real; the author denies that.
4
Reflect on the dialectic
Ask yourself: what would the objector need to show to make the objection work? And why does the author's response successfully block it?
This shows how theological arguments often hinge on metaphysical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : هذا معارض بأنه أي الوجوب نسبة، والنسبة متأخرة عن المنتسبين قطعاً فيكون الواجب متأخراً عن ماهية الواجب، فلا يكون عينها بل زائداً عليها، لأنا نقول : إنما حكمنا بكونه نفس الماهية لا مطلقاً بل على تقدير كونه موجوداً، وكونه نسبة ينافي الفرض المذكور، وهو كونه موجوداً لأن النسب عندنا أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها، فلا يكون كلامكم معارضاً لكلامنا.
It cannot be said: This is contradicted by [the fact] that al-wujūb (necessity) is a relation, and the relation is posterior to the two relata necessarily, so the wājib (necessary being) would be posterior to the essence of the wājib, thus it would not be identical to it but rather additional to it. But we say: We only judged it to be identical to the essence not absolutely, but on the assumption that it exists. And its being a relation contradicts the aforementioned assumption, which is its being existent, because relations in our view are mental considerations with no existence. So your statement does not contradict our statement.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if necessity (wujub) is a relation, then the necessary being would be posterior to its essence, implying that necessity is distinct and not identical. The author responds that the claim of identity is conditional on existence, and the objection's premise that necessity is a relation contradicts the assumption that it is existent, since relations are merely mental considerations. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الوجوب
النسبة
الوارب
الأمور الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if wujub is a relation, it must be a real entity distinct from the essence.
Correction: The author clarifies that relations are merely mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) with no external existence, so wujub as a relation does not have real existence that would make it distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the author's response to the objection that wajib is a relation?
A) Wajib is not a relation because it is identical to the essence.
B) Wajib is a relation but it does not affect identity.
C) The objection is invalid because relations are mental, not external.
D) The objection is invalid because the identity is absolute.

Key Takeaway
The identity of necessity with essence is only asserted under the assumption of existence, and the objector's claim that necessity is a relation fails because relations are merely mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objector assumed necessity is a relation. But we learned that relations are mental. Look at your own beliefs: are you holding onto any 'mental considerations' about Allah that you treat as real? For example, do you imagine Allah's 'hearing' as something added to His essence in your mind? This lesson demands you to purge such notions. How will you remind yourself that all attributes are identical to His essence?
?
The author said 'not absolutely, but on the assumption that it exists.' This condition is crucial. In your daily life, when you say 'Allah is knowing', do you truly feel that His knowledge is His essence, or do you unconsciously think of knowledge as something separate? The Fourth Argument: Wajib Cannot Be Shared Between Two
The author argues that wajib cannot be shared because it is identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity (wujub) of the Necessary Being cannot be shared between two beings, and how this argument relies on the identity of necessity with essence.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be shared because sharing would force composition of essence and specification.

The fourth argument against the possibility of two necessary beings is a subtle argument based on the nature of necessity itself. If necessity were shared, it would be like a common essence shared by two beings, requiring a distinguishing specification to separate them. This would force composition in the Necessary Being, which is impossible. Let's examine the argument and a key comment on its scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the core logic
The argument begins by stating that necessity is identical to the essence. If it were shared between two beings, it would be the same essence of both. Grasp this key premise.
3
Identify the problem of specification
Two things sharing an essence must be distinguished by a specification (ta'ayyun). This would necessitate composition from essence and specification, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
4
Consider the commentator's nuance
The commentator notes that this conclusion follows on the philosopher's view that specification is an existent part, but not on the mutakallimun's view that specification is non-existential and external to the essence. Reflect on this nuance.
This nuance shows how the argument's force depends on broader metaphysical assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أنه لا يكون الوجوب مشتركاً بين اثنين لأنه نفس الماهية فلو كان مشتركاً بينهما لكان نفس ماهيتهما، والمشتركان في الماهية لا بد أن يتمايزا بتعين فيلزم حينئذ تركيبهما من الماهية والتعين. قوله : فيلزم تركيبهما على تقدير جزئية التعين ووجوديته كما هو مذهب الحكيم، وأما عند المتكلمين القائلين بأنه عدمي خارج عن الماهية فلا.
The fourth [argument]: That al-wujūb (necessity) is not shared between two because it is identical to the essence. For if it were shared between them, it would be the same essence of both; and two things sharing an essence must be distinguished by a specification (ta'ayyun), which would then necessitate their composition from [shared] essence and specification. [The commentator says:] 'Then their composition is necessitated' on the assumption that specification is a part and existent, as is the doctrine of the philosopher; but according to the mutakallimun who say it is non-existential and external to the essence, it does not.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that necessity is identical to the essence. If necessity were shared, two necessary beings would share the same necessity, implying they share the same essence, and would need a differentiating specification, leading to composition. The commentator adds the nuance that this argument holds on the philosophers' view that specification is an existent part, but not on the mutakallimun's view that it is non-existential.

Key Terms
الوجوب
المشترك
الماهية
التعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think necessity could be a universal shared among multiple essences, like a common attribute.
Correction: The argument shows that because necessity is identical to the essence, sharing necessity would mean sharing essence, which then forces composition when combined with the need for specification.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the fourth argument according to the commentator's refinement?
A) Wajib is shared but with different specifications.
B) Wajib cannot be shared because it leads to composition, but only if specification is an existent part.
C) Wajib is identical to the specification.
D) Wajib is non-existential.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument demonstrates that sharing necessity would necessitate composition of the Necessary Being, but its validity depends on the metaphysical status of specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah cannot be composite, then what does that imply about how you approach understanding His attributes? Are you willing to accept that your finite mind cannot fully grasp His oneness, or do you still try to reduce Him to categories you understand?
?
This argument shows that sharing necessity would break divine simplicity. In your own life, where are you tempted to 'share' your devotion to Allah with something else, making your heart composite? What specification (ta'ayyun) do you use to distinguish your loyalties?
?
The mutakallimun avoided the argument's conclusion by denying specification is part of the essence. When you find a theological difficulty, do you seek an intellectually honest resolution, or do you simply dismiss the argument to avoid discomfort?

5 min
Wajub is Identical to Essence: The Fourth Argument
Al-Ansari's fourth argument that wujub cannot be shared

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that wujub cannot be shared because it is identical to the essence, and how the objection that wujub might be an accident is refuted.

Core Idea
Sharing wujub would force composition in the Necessary, which is impossible.

Al-Ansari presents a logical proof that wujub (necessity) is not a shared attribute between two necessary beings. The argument hinges on the identity of wujub with the essence, leading to an impossibility of composition. This lesson unpacks the argument and addresses a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
Trace the argument: if wujub is shared between two beings, it would be identical to each essence. Then they must be distinguished by individuation, leading to composition of essence and individuation, which is impossible for the Necessary.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection claims wujub could be an accident, not identical to essence, thus avoiding composition. Note how this challenges the premise.
4
Understand the response
The response argues that the claim is about wujub being an existent attribute; if it were existent, it must be identical to essence, so the argument still holds.
The response assumes wujub is existential; the later section handles non-existential.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this argument is important for proving the oneness of God: it rules out multiple necessary beings sharing the same attribute of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أنه لا يكون) الوجوب ( مشتركاً بين اثنين لأنه نفس الماهية) فلو كان مشتركاً بينهما لكان نفس ماهيتهما، ) والمشتركان في الماهية لا بد أن يتمايزا بتعين فيلزم حينئذ (تركبهما) من الماهية والتعين ( وأنه محال لما مر من امتناع تركب الواجب ( لا يقال : لا نسلم أنه نفس الماهية لجواز أن يكون عارضاً لها فلا يلزم تركب الواجب ( لأنا نقول : المدعى ) هو ( أنه لا يكون ) الوجوب ) وجودياً مشتركاً ، وقد بينا أنه لو كان وجودياً كان نفس الماهية)، والأظهر أن يحال هذا الحكم على برهان التوحيد، ليظهر امتناع الاشتراك مطلقاً. قوله : ( مطلقاً ) أي سواء كان عارضاً أو نفس الماهية. قوله : ( والأظهر أن يحال هذا إلخ لبعض المتأخرين هاهنا إشكال قوي وهو أنه كيف يحيله على برهان التوحيد ولم يذكر ثمة إلا دليلين على نفي تعدد الواجب على طريقة الحكماء، وكلاهما مبني على كون الوجوب ثبوتياً ونفس الماهية كما صرح به هناك، ودليلين أيضاً على طريقة المتكلمين على نفي تعدد الإله، ولا تعرض فيهما للوجوب ونفي تعدده، وغاية ما يقال بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد بالبرهان المذكور في غير هذا الكتاب أن الوجوب الذاتي أخص أوصاف الباري تعالى وأن الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية وبالجملة هو معدن لكل كمال ومبعد عن كل نقصان كما صرحوا به فلما ثبت بدلیل المتكلمين انتفاء تعدد الإله ثبت انتفاء تعدد الواجب، سواء كان الوجوب وجودياً أو عدمياً، لأن الاشتراك في الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية المقتضية للألوهية، والحاصل أن الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الألوهية، وتعدده تعدد الآلهة والدليل الدال على انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم .
And the fourth: that necessity (al-wujub) is not shared between two because it is identical to the essence (al-mahiya). For if it were shared between them, it would be identical to their essences. And two things that share an essence must be distinguished by individuation (al-ta'yin), thus composition (al-tarkib) of essence and individuation would follow. And that is impossible due to what has passed of the impossibility of composition in the Necessary. It is not said: we do not accept that it is identical to the essence, because it is possible that it is an accident of it, so composition of the Necessary does not follow. For we say: the claim is that necessity is not an existent shared attribute, and we have shown that if it were existent it would be identical to the essence. And it is most apparent to refer this judgment to the proof of tawhid, to show the impossibility of sharing absolutely. His statement: 'absolutely' means whether it is an accident or identical to the essence. His statement: 'And it is most apparent to refer this, etc.' Some later scholars here have a strong objection, which is: how can he refer it to the proof of tawhid when he mentioned there only two proofs for negating the multiplicity of the Necessary according to the method of the philosophers, both based on the fact that wujub is an existential attribute and identical to the essence as he explicitly stated there, and also two proofs according to the method of the theologians for negating the multiplicity of the god, and they do not address wujub and the negation of its multiplicity? The utmost that can be said, after conceding that by the aforementioned proof in other than this book is meant that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Exalted Creator, and sharing in the most specific attribute necessitates sharing in the essence, and in short He is the source of every perfection and far from every deficiency as they have explicitly stated. So when the negation of the multiplicity of the god is established by the theologians' proof, the negation of the multiplicity of the Necessary is established, whether wujub is existential or non-existential, because sharing in self-necessity necessitates sharing in the essence that entails divinity (al-uluhiyya). The outcome is that self-necessity entails divinity, and its multiplicity is the multiplicity of gods, and the proof that negates the consequent (al-lazim) negates the antecedent (al-malzum).
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (رابعها) in a series of proofs that necessity (al-wujub) is identical to the divine essence and cannot be shared by multiple beings. The argument proceeds as follows: if wujub were shared between two beings, it would be identical to each of their essences. But two things sharing an essence must be distinguished by individuation (al-ta'yin), leading to composition (al-tarkib) of essence and individuation. Composition in the Necessary is impossible, as established earlier. An objection is raised: perhaps wujub is not identical to the essence but an accident of it, so composition does not follow. The answer is that the claim being made is that wujub is not an existent shared attribute; it has already been shown that if it were existent it would be identical to the essence. Therefore, the impossibility of sharing stands. The author then suggests that this entire judgment should be referred to the proof of tawhid (oneness of God) to show the absolute impossibility of sharing, regardless of whether wujub is considered an accident or identical to essence. A later commentator raises a strong objection: how can the proof of tawhid suffice, since in that proof only two arguments were given against multiplicity—one based on wujub being existential and identical to essence (by the philosophers), and two others from theologians negating multiple gods without directly addressing wujub? The answer is that the intended proof of tawhid is not limited to those arguments; rather, it relies on the principle that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Creator, and sharing in this attribute entails sharing in the essence that necessitates divinity. Thus, if the existence of multiple gods is proven impossible (by the theologians' arguments), then the existence of multiple necessary beings is also impossible, because self-necessity entails divinity and its multiplicity would imply multiple gods. This holds whether wujub is considered existential or non-existential, because the key is the logical entailment: the consequent (divinity) is negated, so the antecedent (self-necessity shared) is also negated.

Key Terms
وجوب
ماهية
تعين
تركيب
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument fails if wujub is considered an accident of the essence, because then composition does not follow.
Correction: The response clarifies that the argument is about wujub as an existent attribute; if it were existent, it must be identical to essence, preserving the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the key step that leads to impossibility in the fourth argument?
A) If wujub is shared, it would be an accident of the essence.
B) Sharing wujub forces composition of essence and individuation in the Necessary.
C) The objection proves that wujub is not identical to essence.
D) The philosophers proved wujub is not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument proves that wujub cannot be shared because sharing would force composition in the Necessary, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that wujub is unique to Allah and cannot be shared. Where in your life do you act as if something else—a person, a possession, or a status—is truly necessary for your happiness or survival? How can you dismantle that attachment today?
?
If composition in the Necessary is impossible, what does that require of your heart's focus? In what areas are you trying to serve both Allah and your own desires, creating a 'composed' loyalty? What one intentional step can you take this week to unify your intentions?
?
Al-Ansari's argument shows that sharing wujub forces composition and impossibility. Examine your own heart: Do you give Allah's rights—like trust, fear, and hope—to other beings? Identify one relationship where you have unconsciously delegated ultimate reliance. What concrete action will you take to restore that reliance solely to Allah?

Referral to the Proof of Tawhid
Al-Ansari's referral of the fourth argument to the proof of tawhid and subsequent clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth argument can be referred to the proof of tawhid, and how the proof of tawhid establishes the impossibility of sharing wujub even if wujub is non-existential.

Core Idea
Self-necessity entails divinity, so negating multiple gods negates multiple necessary beings.

After presenting the fourth argument, al-Ansari suggests that the impossibility of sharing wujub should be referred to the proof of tawhid (oneness of God). This lesson explores the strong objection raised by later scholars and how the proof of tawhid ultimately resolves the issue by linking self-necessity to divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully. Note that it includes the referral, an objection, and a resolution.
2
Understand the referral
Al-Ansari says the judgment of impossibility should be referred to the proof of tawhid to show absolute impossibility, regardless of whether wujub is an accident or identical to essence.
This expands the argument beyond the existential case.
3
Grasp the objection
A later scholar objects: how can the proof of tawhid be sufficient? It only contains two philosophical proofs based on wujub being existential and two theological proofs against multiple gods, not directly addressing wujub.
4
Learn the resolution
The resolution is that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Creator; sharing it entails sharing essence, which entails divinity. Thus, negating multiple gods (by theologians' proof) negates multiple necessary beings.
5
Connect to the overall argument
This shows that the fourth argument holds irrespective of the ontological status of wujub, because it ultimately depends on the link between self-necessity and divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : أنه لا يكون) الوجوب ( مشتركاً بين اثنين لأنه نفس الماهية) فلو كان مشتركاً بينهما لكان نفس ماهيتهما، ) والمشتركان في الماهية لا بد أن يتمايزا بتعين فيلزم حينئذ (تركبهما) من الماهية والتعين ( وأنه محال لما مر من امتناع تركب الواجب ( لا يقال : لا نسلم أنه نفس الماهية لجواز أن يكون عارضاً لها فلا يلزم تركب الواجب ( لأنا نقول : المدعى ) هو ( أنه لا يكون ) الوجوب ) وجودياً مشتركاً ، وقد بينا أنه لو كان وجودياً كان نفس الماهية)، والأظهر أن يحال هذا الحكم على برهان التوحيد، ليظهر امتناع الاشتراك مطلقاً. قوله : ( مطلقاً ) أي سواء كان عارضاً أو نفس الماهية. قوله : ( والأظهر أن يحال هذا إلخ لبعض المتأخرين هاهنا إشكال قوي وهو أنه كيف يحيله على برهان التوحيد ولم يذكر ثمة إلا دليلين على نفي تعدد الواجب على طريقة الحكماء، وكلاهما مبني على كون الوجوب ثبوتياً ونفس الماهية كما صرح به هناك، ودليلين أيضاً على طريقة المتكلمين على نفي تعدد الإله، ولا تعرض فيهما للوجوب ونفي تعدده، وغاية ما يقال بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد بالبرهان المذكور في غير هذا الكتاب أن الوجوب الذاتي أخص أوصاف الباري تعالى وأن الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية وبالجملة هو معدن لكل كمال ومبعد عن كل نقصان كما صرحوا به فلما ثبت بدلیل المتكلمين انتفاء تعدد الإله ثبت انتفاء تعدد الواجب، سواء كان الوجوب وجودياً أو عدمياً، لأن الاشتراك في الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية المقتضية للألوهية، والحاصل أن الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الألوهية، وتعدده تعدد الآلهة والدليل الدال على انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم .
And the fourth: that necessity (al-wujub) is not shared between two because it is identical to the essence (al-mahiya). For if it were shared between them, it would be identical to their essences. And two things that share an essence must be distinguished by individuation (al-ta'yin), thus composition (al-tarkib) of essence and individuation would follow. And that is impossible due to what has passed of the impossibility of composition in the Necessary. It is not said: we do not accept that it is identical to the essence, because it is possible that it is an accident of it, so composition of the Necessary does not follow. For we say: the claim is that necessity is not an existent shared attribute, and we have shown that if it were existent it would be identical to the essence. And it is most apparent to refer this judgment to the proof of tawhid, to show the impossibility of sharing absolutely. His statement: 'absolutely' means whether it is an accident or identical to the essence. His statement: 'And it is most apparent to refer this, etc.' Some later scholars here have a strong objection, which is: how can he refer it to the proof of tawhid when he mentioned there only two proofs for negating the multiplicity of the Necessary according to the method of the philosophers, both based on the fact that wujub is an existential attribute and identical to the essence as he explicitly stated there, and also two proofs according to the method of the theologians for negating the multiplicity of the god, and they do not address wujub and the negation of its multiplicity? The utmost that can be said, after conceding that by the aforementioned proof in other than this book is meant that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Exalted Creator, and sharing in the most specific attribute necessitates sharing in the essence, and in short He is the source of every perfection and far from every deficiency as they have explicitly stated. So when the negation of the multiplicity of the god is established by the theologians' proof, the negation of the multiplicity of the Necessary is established, whether wujub is existential or non-existential, because sharing in self-necessity necessitates sharing in the essence that entails divinity (al-uluhiyya). The outcome is that self-necessity entails divinity, and its multiplicity is the multiplicity of gods, and the proof that negates the consequent (al-lazim) negates the antecedent (al-malzum).
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (رابعها) in a series of proofs that necessity (al-wujub) is identical to the divine essence and cannot be shared by multiple beings. The argument proceeds as follows: if wujub were shared between two beings, it would be identical to each of their essences. But two things sharing an essence must be distinguished by individuation (al-ta'yin), leading to composition (al-tarkib) of essence and individuation. Composition in the Necessary is impossible, as established earlier. An objection is raised: perhaps wujub is not identical to the essence but an accident of it, so composition does not follow. The answer is that the claim being made is that wujub is not an existent shared attribute; it has already been shown that if it were existent it would be identical to the essence. Therefore, the impossibility of sharing stands. The author then suggests that this entire judgment should be referred to the proof of tawhid (oneness of God) to show the absolute impossibility of sharing, regardless of whether wujub is considered an accident or identical to essence. A later commentator raises a strong objection: how can the proof of tawhid suffice, since in that proof only two arguments were given against multiplicity—one based on wujub being existential and identical to essence (by the philosophers), and two others from theologians negating multiple gods without directly addressing wujub? The answer is that the intended proof of tawhid is not limited to those arguments; rather, it relies on the principle that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Creator, and sharing in this attribute entails sharing in the essence that necessitates divinity. Thus, if the existence of multiple gods is proven impossible (by the theologians' arguments), then the existence of multiple necessary beings is also impossible, because self-necessity entails divinity and its multiplicity would imply multiple gods. This holds whether wujub is considered existential or non-existential, because the key is the logical entailment: the consequent (divinity) is negated, so the antecedent (self-necessity shared) is also negated.

Key Terms
برهان التوحيد
وجوب ذاتي
ألوهية
لازم
ملزوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof of tawhid is irrelevant to negating multiple necessary beings because it only addresses gods, not the attribute of necessity.
Correction: The correction is that self-necessity is the most specific attribute entailing divinity, so negating multiple gods logically negates multiple necessary beings.

Checkpoint
What is the key relationship that allows the proof of tawhid to establish the impossibility of sharing wujub?
A) Wujub is identical to essence in all cases.
B) Self-necessity entails divinity.
C) The philosophers proved that wujub is existential.
D) The theologians proved that there is only one god.

Key Takeaway
The proof of tawhid ultimately establishes the impossibility of sharing wujub by linking self-necessity to divinity, making the argument independent of the nature of wujub.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about God without examining whether your proof holds for all perspectives, just as the later scholar assumed the proof of tawhid only worked for one view? Where else in your faith might you be relying on a partial proof? Will you commit today to seeking a more universal understanding?
?
This lesson shows that negating the consequent (divinity) forces you to negate the antecedent (self-necessity). What if a doubt about God's oneness creeps into your heart? Are you willing to trace that doubt to its root in your understanding of God's nature, or do you ignore it? How will you apply this logical tool to purify your conviction?
?
The argument here links self-necessity to divinity — one implies the other. Do you live as if your belief in one God logically demands that you see His necessity in every aspect of your life? Or do you compartmentalize your faith, allowing areas where you act as if other 'gods' (desires, people, status) are necessary? What one step can you take today to align your priorities with the logical conclusion that nothing deserves ultimate necessity except Him?

The Free Agent's Preference Without a Motivator
Defines the free agent and distinguishes possible from impossible preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between a free agent's active preference (tarjīḥ) and a passive preponderance (tarajjuḥ), and why the former is possible while the latter is not.

Core Idea
A free agent can prefer without a motive; impossible is passive preponderance without cause.

This passage tackles a key question in Islamic theology: how can we affirm that God acts freely without requiring external motivators? The author distinguishes between two concepts often conflated: the active preferring by a free agent, and the passive preponderance of one possibility over another. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Ashʿarite position on divine will and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the two cases
The author presents two scenarios: preference (tarjīḥ) without a motive, and preponderance (tarajjuḥ) without a cause. Locate these in the Arabic text.
Notice the difference in Arabic roots: ج-ر-ح vs ر-ج-ح.
2
Analyze the author's reasoning
The author argues that the first case is possible because the agent is free, while the second is impossible because it would constitute an effect without a cause. Trace the logical steps.
He says 'you already know its impossibility by necessity' – referring to the principle of causality.
3
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why this distinction is crucial for defending God's free will and rejecting necessitarianism.
This is a cornerstone of Ashʿarite occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختيار.
الحوادث قديم ) مختار عندنا)، وفعله تابع لإرادته وتعلق إرادته بتخصيص الحدوث ببعض الأوقات مع تساويها لا يحتاج إلى داع بل له أن يختار أحد مقدوريه المتساويين على الآخر بلا سبب يدعوه إليه، فإن ذلك هو الكمال في ( والترجيح) الصادر من الفاعل لأحد مقدوريه على الآخر (لا لداع) يدعوه إلى اختيار ذلك المقدور ( غير الوقوع) أي وقوع أحد المتسايين (بلا سبب) مؤثر، والثاني هو المحال، لأنه ترجح أحد المتساويين من طرفي الممكن بلا سبب مرجح من خارج وقد عرفت بطلانه بالضرورة وأما الأول فليس بمحال لأنه ترجيح من غير مرجح أي من غير داع يدعوه لا من غير ذات متصف بالترجيح ولا استحالة فيه؛ لأن المؤثر إذا كان مختاراً، فهو يرجح كيف يشاء.
The Free Agent: The temporal events are eternal (the free agent according to us), and His action is dependent on His will, and the attachment of His will to specifying occurrence in some times, while they are equal, does not need a motive; rather, He can choose one of His two equal capabilities over the other without a cause calling Him to it. For that is perfection. And the preference (al-tarjīḥ) emanating from the agent for one of His two capabilities over the other (without a motive) calling Him to choose that capability (other than occurrence) means the occurrence of one of the two equals (without a cause) efficient. And the second is impossible, because it is the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) of one equal from the two sides of the possible without a preponderating cause from outside, and you already know its impossibility by necessity. As for the first, it is not impossible, because it is a preferring (tarjīḥ) without a preponderator, i.e., without a motive calling Him, not without a self described with preferring, and there is no impossibility in it; because the effecter, when he is free, he prefers as he wills.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the free agent (al-mukhtār) in the context of divine action. It states that God's action is dependent on His will, and the attachment of His will to specifying the occurrence of events at particular times, even when those times are equally possible, does not require a motivating cause. Rather, God can choose one of two equally possible actions without any cause prompting Him. This, the author says, is perfection. The passage then distinguishes between two cases: (1) the agent preferring one of His two equal capabilities without a motive (tarjīḥ bi-lā dā‘in), and (2) one of two equals becoming preponderant without a preponderating cause (tarajjuḥ bi-lā sabab). The first is possible, because the effecter is free and can prefer as He wills. The second is impossible, because it would mean an effect occurring without a cause, which is known to be impossible by necessity. The key point is that the impossibility lies in the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) occurring without a cause, not in the act of preferring (tarjīḥ) itself, as long as the agent is free.

Key Terms
المختار
الترجيح
الترجح
الداعي
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any choice without a motive is impossible because it would imply a random event without cause.
Correction: The author clarifies that the impossibility is only in preponderance (tarajjuḥ) where the effect occurs without a cause. Active preference (tarjīḥ) by a free agent does not require a motive; the agent's freedom itself is sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what distinguishes possible preference from impossible preponderance?
A) Possible preference involves a cause, while impossible preponderance does not.
B) Possible preference is an act of a free agent, while impossible preponderance is a passive occurrence without a cause.
C) Possible preference requires a motive, while impossible preponderance does not.
D) Both are equally impossible.

Key Takeaway
A free agent's active preference without a motive is possible and a sign of perfection, while passive preponderance without a cause is impossible by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's will is sufficient reason for everything—or do you secretly demand that God justify His choices to you? What would change if you truly embraced that His preference is perfection, not arbitrariness?
?
When you face two equal goods and must choose, do you trust that your free choice (even with no clear reason) is part of your dignity as a creature made in God's image? Or do you freeze, feeling that every choice must have a clear motive?
?
If God's active preference without a motive is possible and perfect, then why do you insist that every event in your life must have a visible cause before you trust it as good? What does that say about your view of God's freedom?

The Objection of Indifference in the Divine Will and Its Resolution
Objection against divine will and resolutions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the specificity of the divine will leads to infinite regress or an uncaused cause, and the key resolutions proposed by theologians, particularly the distinction between real existents and mental considerations.

Core Idea
The divine will's specificity is not a problem if we distinguish mental considerations from real causes.

This passage addresses a classic objection against the doctrine of an eternal and determinate divine will. If God's will is one and eternal, why does He choose to create the world at one time rather than another? The objector claims this leads to an infinite regress of wills or an uncaused cause. In response, theologians offer several nuanced solutions, involving the nature of attachments and the distinction between real and mental entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the structure of the objection. Notice how the objector presents a dilemma.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection's dilemma
Find the question posed at the beginning and the two possible answers that lead to absurdities. This is the core of the objection.
The two horns: infinite regress or uncaused preference.
3
Examine the counter-response
The opponent suggests the will is one but attachments vary. See how this leads to another regress.
This shows the objection is robust.
4
Analyze the four resolutions
The commentator lists several ways to resolve the regress. Identify each and note the commentator's favored solution.
Pay attention to the distinction between real and mental.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Understand why mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) do not require causes in the same way as real existents. This is central to the Ash'ari position.
This connects to broader principles of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاته توجه أن يقال : لم اتصف بإحدى الإرادتين دون الأخرى؟ فإن أسند هذه ترجیح الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى نقلنا الكلام إليها، ولزم تسلسل الإرادات . وإن لم يسند إلى شيء فقد ترجح أحد المتساويين على الآخر بلا سبب . فإن قيل: الإرادة واحدة لكن يتعدد تعلقها بحسب المرادات :قلنا فيلزم حينئذ التسلسل في التعلقات . قوله : ( فقد ترجح أحد المتساويين أعني وجود الإرادة على عدمها بلا سبب، فيلزم وقوع الممكن بلا علة . قوله : ( فيلزم حينئذ التسلسل إن استند تعلق الإرادة إلى تعلق آخر، وإلا يلزم وقوع التعلق بلا سبب. وقد عرفت اندفاعه إما باختيار أن التعلق أزلي ولا تخلف أو باختيار أنه متجدد ومخصص وقوعه نفس الإرادة أو أنه واقع بلا سبب ولا يلزم من جواز وقوع الأمور الاعتبارية بلا سبب جواز وجود الممكن بلا سبب وهذا هو مختار صدر الشريعة في التوضيح، وهو في غاية المتانة. وقد حققناه في حواشينا على المقدمات الأربعة، أو بالتزام التسلسل في التعلقات لكونها اعتبارية، والقول بأن التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية النفس الأمرية أيضاً محال لجريان برهان التطبيق، فسيجيء الكلام فيه إن شاء الله تعالى .
His essence is addressed so that it is said: Why is He characterized with one of the two wills rather than the other? For if He attributes this preference of the will to another will, we transfer the discussion to it, and an infinite regress of wills follows. And if He does not attribute it to anything, then one of the two equals has been preferred over the other without a cause. Then if it is said: The will is one but its attachments vary according to the objects of will; we say: Then in that case an infinite regress in the attachments follows. His words: (Then a preference of one of the two equals has occurred) meaning the existence of the will over its non-existence without a cause, so the occurrence of the contingent without a cause follows. His words: (Then in that case an infinite regress follows) if the attachment of the will is based on another attachment, otherwise the occurrence of the attachment without a cause follows. And you have known its refutation either by choosing that the attachment is eternal and does not change, or by choosing that it is renewed and its occurrence is specified by the will itself, or that it occurs without a cause and the permissibility of the occurrence of mental matters without a cause does not necessitate the permissibility of the existence of the contingent without a cause. This is the choice of Sadr al-Shari'ah in al-Tawdih, and it is extremely solid. And we have verified it in our glosses on the Four Premises. Or by accepting infinite regress in attachments because they are mental, and the statement that infinite regress in mental things of the self-command is also impossible due to the running of the proof of application, the discussion of which will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Ash'ari perspective regarding the divine will. The objector asks: why does God will one thing rather than another? If the preference is due to another will, there is infinite regress. If without cause, then something occurs without a cause, which is impossible. The opponent then suggests that the will is one but its attachments (ta'alluqat) vary; this leads to infinite regress in attachments. The commentator responds by summarizing several resolutions: (1) the attachment is eternal and does not change; (2) it is renewed but its specification is the very will; (3) it occurs without cause, permissible for mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) but not for real existents; (4) accept infinite regress in attachments because they are mental. The commentator favors Sadr al-Shari'ah's view (option 3) and notes that infinite regress in mental things is also impossible by the proof of correspondence (burhan al-tatbiq), to be discussed later. The key nuance is that the need for a cause applies to real existents, not mental considerations.

إرادة
تعلق
تسلسل
أمور اعتبارية
برهان التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mental considerations require causes just like real existents.
Correction: The passage clarifies that mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) can occur without a cause, unlike real existents, which require one.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a proposed resolution to the infinite regress of the divine will's attachments?
A) The attachment is eternal and does not change.
B) The attachment is renewed and its specification is the will itself.
C) The attachment is a real entity in the external world.
D) The attachment occurs without a cause, permissible for mental considerations.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the divine will requires a cause is resolved by distinguishing between real entities and mental considerations, where mental considerations do not require causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the divine will's specificity is resolved by distinguishing between real and mental considerations. But what about your own will? When you choose one thing over another, do you ever stop to ask yourself whether your preferences have a real cause or are they also 'mental considerations'? And if they are the latter, what does that say about your responsibility for your choices?
?
The objector in this passage demands a cause for God's specific will. But consider: Do you treat God's actions as fully understandable, or do you, in your heart, demand that God explain Himself to you? This lesson shows that some things are beyond causality as we know it. Are you willing to submit to that mystery, or do you still expect a reason for everything?
?
Sadr al-Shari'ah's resolution says that mental considerations can occur without a cause. Think of your own ingrained assumptions—like the assumption that you deserve something good. Is that a mental consideration or rooted in reality? If it has no cause in reality, will you still hold on to it, or will you let it go in light of what you have just read?
