Al-Āmidī on the Eternal and the Free Agent
Discussion of al-Āmidī's view on eternal depending on free agent and attributes as requirements of essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Āmidī's view that the eternal can depend on a free agent and why divine attributes are not created effects.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not effects; they are requirements of the essence like existence itself.

This passage from al-Mawāqif presents a debate on whether the eternal (God's attributes or the world) can be dependent on a free agent (mukhtār) versus a necessitating cause (mūjib). Al-Āmidī is reported to have permitted the former, and the commentator explains that attributes are not produced after existence but are inherent to the essence. We will explore these nuanced arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from the initial statement to the commentary on attributes.
The passage is dense and has high OCR risk; some words may be corrupted.
2
Identify the two issues
The passage discusses two matters: the permissibility of the eternal depending on a free agent, and the nature of divine attributes as not being created effects. Find where each is addressed.
3
Understand al-Āmidī's position
Al-Āmidī permits the eternal depending on the free agent. Look for the hand-and-ring analogy and how it illustrates precedence by essence.
The hand-and-ring example shows that cause can precede effect by essence, not time.
4
Grasp the commentary on attributes
The commentator argues that attributes are 'muqtaḍayāt al-dhāt' (requirements of the essence) like existence. They are not effects because the level of creation is after the level of existence.
This addresses the misconception that attributes are created entities separate from God.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for divine simplicity and the nature of God's relationship with His attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( ولقد عثرت في كلام القوم على منع الأمرين ( يعني عدم جواز استناد القديم إلى المختار وجواز استناده إلى الموجب ( أما استناده إلى المختار فجوزه الآمدي وقال : سبق الإيجاد قصداً) على وجود

قوله : ( أمور لفظية لا معنوية لأن هذه الأقوال صريحة في استناد الأمور الأزلية إلى العلة سواء أطلقوا عليها القديم أو لا، وفي استناد الصفات القديمة إلى العلة سواء قالوا إنها غيرها أولا، وأقول الكلام في استناد القديم إلى الموجب بمعنى كونه أثراً صادراً عنه مستفيداً للوجود منه، والأحوال ليس لها وجود أصالة حتى تستند باعتباره إلى العلة الموجدة بل هي موجودة بتبع صاحبها والتعليل هاهنا باعتبار أنفسها فإن العالمية نسبة بين العالم والمعلوم يتصف بها العالم بسبب اتصافه بالعلم، فلا استناد لها في وجودها إلى العلة المؤثرة فيه، وصفاته تعالى لما كانت مقتضيات ذاته كالوجود كانت في مرتبة الوجود في اقتضاء الذات إياها وكونها لازمة له، فلا يتصور كونها آثاراً صادرة عنه، لأن مرتبة الإيجاد بعد مرتبة الوجود، فلا تكون مستندة إلى علة موجدة نعم يكون من مقتضيات ذاته كالوجود، وهذا معنى قولهم : إنها ليست غير الذات أي أموراً يمكن أنفكاكها عنه في الوجود بأن يكون وجودها بعد مرتبة وجوده تعالى فتكون آثاراً مستندة إليه تعالى بل حالها حال الوجود في كونها مقتضى الذات . قوله : ( وقال سبق إلخ) هذا الكلام تصوير منه لجواز كون القديم أثر المختار بعدم الفرق

قوله : ( يعني عدم جواز استناد القديم إلى (المختار في المباحث المشرقية في الفصل التاسع والأربعين من الفن الخامس تصریح بجواز استناد القديم إلى المختار وقد نقل مثله عن بطلميوس.

قوله: (فجوزه الآمدي قال في شرح المقاصد : وما نقل في المواقف من الآمدي لا يوجد أنه لا في كتاب أبكار الأفكار إلا ما قال على سبيل الاعتراض من يمتنع أن يكون وجود العالم أزلياً مستنداً إلى الواجب تعالى ويكونان معاً في الوجود لا تقدم إلا بالذات، كما في حركة اليد لا والخاتم، وهو يشعر بابتنائه على كون الواجب تعالى مختاراً لا موجباً، ولهذا مثل بحركة اليد والخاتم، واقتصر في الجواب على منع السند قائلاً : لا نسلم استناد حركة الخاتم إلى حركة اليد، بل هما معلولان لأمر خارج وفيه بحث؛ إذ لا وجه لجعل ما ذكره الآمدي اعتراضاً، إلا إذا كان المراد تجويز استناد العالم على تقدير أزليته إلى القادر المختار، فإنه لا نزاع في جواز استناده على ذلك التقدير إلى الموجب، وجعل الاعتراض راجعاً إلى قاعدة الاختيار يأباه سياق الكلام، على أنها مبرهن عليها فلا وجه للاقتصار في الجواب على منع السند حينئذ. والحق: ما ذكره المصنف، وفي الاقتصار المذكور إشارة إلى قوة الاعتراض. ومن هاهنا قال المصنف : جوزه الآمدي، وأما التمثيل بحركة اليد والخاتم ففي مجرد أن تقدم العلة بالذات لا في الإيجاب. قوله : ( وقال سبق الإيجاد قصداً إلخ) هذه العبارة غير وافية بالمقصود، لأنها تدل على
The author said: ("I have come across in the speech of the people the prevention of two matters") meaning the impermissibility of the eternal being based on the free agent (mukhtār) and the permissibility of its being based on the necessitating cause (mūjib). ("As for its being based on the free agent, al-Āmidī permitted it and said: precedence of creation intentionally over existence")

His saying: ("verbal matters not conceptual") because these statements are explicit about the dependence of eternal things on a cause, whether they call them eternal or not, and about the dependence of eternal attributes on a cause, whether they say they are other than Him or not. I say: the discussion is about the dependence of the eternal on the necessitating cause in the sense of its being an effect emanating from it receiving existence from it. States (aḥwāl) have no existence in themselves to be dependent by virtue of it on the efficient cause; rather they exist as dependent on their possessor. The causation here is with respect to their essences. For the state of being a knower ('ālimiyya) is a relation between the knower and the known, the knower is characterized by it due to being characterized by knowledge. So there is no dependence for its existence on the efficient cause. And His attributes, since they are requirements of His essence like existence, are on the level of existence in terms of the essence requiring them and their being necessary for it. So they cannot be considered effects emanating from Him, because the level of creation is after the level of existence. So they are not dependent on an efficient cause. Rather, they are among the requirements of His essence like existence. This is the meaning of their saying: they are not other than the essence, i.e., things that can be separated from Him in existence, such that their existence is after the level of His existence and thus be effects dependent on Him. Rather, their state is like existence in being a requirement of the essence.

His saying: ("and said precedence...") this speech is his depiction of the permissibility of the eternal being the effect of the free agent without distinction.

His saying: ("meaning the impermissibility of the eternal being based on the free agent") in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, in the forty-ninth chapter of the fifth art, there is an explicit statement about the permissibility of the eternal being based on the free agent, and the like has been transmitted from Ptolemy.

His saying: ("so al-Āmidī permitted it") said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: What is transmitted in al-Mawāqif from al-Āmidī is not found in the book Abkār al-Afkār except what he said by way of objection: "It is impossible that the existence of the world be eternal dependent on the Necessary Being, and they both be together in existence with no precedence except by essence, as in the movement of the hand and the ring." This suggests that it is based on the Necessary Being being free agent not necessitating. Hence he gave the example of hand and ring movement. And he restricted the answer to denying the basis, saying: "We do not accept that the ring's movement depends on the hand's movement; rather, both are effects of an external cause." And there is discussion: there is no reason to take what al-Āmidī said as an objection, unless it was meant to permit the world being based on the powerful free agent on the assumption of its eternity, for there is no dispute about the permissibility of its being based on the necessitating cause on that assumption. And making the objection refer back to the rule of free choice is forbidden by the context of the speech. Moreover, it is proven, so there is no reason to restrict the answer to denying the basis in that case. The truth is what the author mentioned, and in the aforementioned restriction there is an indication of the strength of the objection. From here the author said: al-Āmidī permitted it. As for the example of hand and ring, it is merely that precedence of cause by essence does not necessitate predetermination (ījāb). His saying: ("and said precedence of existence purposefully") etc., this expression is insufficient for the purpose, because it indicates
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of the eternal being based on the free agent (mukhtār) versus the necessitating cause (mūjib). The author finds in the speech of the people (the mutakallimūn) a denial of both: the impermissibility of basing the eternal on the free agent and the permissibility of basing it on the necessitating cause. Then al-Āmidī is reported to have permitted basing the eternal on the free agent. The commentator explains that al-Āmidī's statement is not found in his book except as an objection, and the truth is what the author mentioned. The commentator argues that the attributes of God are not effects in the sense of being created after existence; they are requirements of the essence like existence itself. So they are not dependent on an efficient cause. The discussion continues with al-Āmidī's example of hand and ring movement to illustrate precedence by essence without necessitation. The commentator critiques the response that denies the basis, arguing that the truth is the author's view. The passage ends with a note that al-Āmidī's expression is insufficient.

Key Terms
مختار
موجب
قديم
أثر
مقتضى الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine attributes are created entities separate from God's essence, brought into existence after Him.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that attributes are 'muqtaḍayāt al-dhāt' (requirements of the essence) like existence itself; they are not effects that come into being at a later level.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why are God's attributes not like effects emanating from Him?
A) Because they are temporal
B) Because they are requirements of the essence like existence, and the level of creation is after existence
C) Because they are separate from His essence
D) Because they are caused by another

Key Takeaway
Al-Āmidī's permission of the eternal depending on the free agent is supported by the hand-ring analogy and the claim that divine attributes are not created but are necessary to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are necessary requirements of His essence, then He never 'acquired' knowledge or power—He always was all-knowing and all-powerful. How does this truth change the way you approach Him in your dua and your trust in His knowledge of your situation? This week, catch yourself when you doubt that God fully knows your struggle and intentionally remind yourself that His knowledge is not a later addition but eternal.
?
Al-Āmidī argued that the eternal can depend on a free agent, using the hand-ring analogy. If God's act of creation is not forced by necessity but flows from His free choice, then your existence is not an accident or a mere consequence. How does this affect your sense of purpose? Are you living as someone deliberately brought into being by a wise and free Creator? Commit to one action today that aligns with that purpose.
?
The lesson emphasizes that attributes are not 'other than the essence' in a way that suggests separation. Yet many people think of God's mercy as something He 'decides' to have at certain times. But mercy is as eternal as His existence. Will you let that truth sink into your heart when you fear His judgment? Recall a specific sin you feel guilty about and now reframe your repentance: knowing that His mercy has always been there, not as an afterthought.

Essential Priority in Divine Creation
Defines two types of creation and their causal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine creation can be simultaneous with its effect in time while being prior in essence, reconciling divine freedom with the world's pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Cause and effect can be simultaneous in time yet distinct in essence.

This passage from al-Mawaqif tackles a subtle theological point: how can God create the world voluntarily if the world is eternal? The author, al-Jurjani, argues that priority in essence, not time, is what matters for causation. He distinguishes two types of creation and uses the analogy of hand and ring to illustrate essential priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the key terms: سبق بالذات and سبق بالزمان.
2
Distinguish the two creations
Identify the two types: الإيجاد الإيجابي (necessary emanation) and الإيجاد القصدي (voluntary creation). Notice the claim that the priority of the first is essential, not temporal.
3
Understand essential priority
Reflect on the meaning of priority in essence (bil-dhāt) as opposed to priority in time (bil-zamān). The author asserts that voluntary creation can also have essential priority while being simultaneous in time.
The hand-and-ring analogy clarifies: the hand's movement is prior in essence to the ring's movement, even though they occur together.
4
Consider the consequence
The author concludes that the world can be pre-eternal (في الأزل) and necessary by God, yet still created by a voluntary agent. This reconciles God's freedom with the world's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلول كسبق الإيجاد إيجاباً، فكما أن ذلك أي سبق الإيجاد الإيجابي سبق بالذات لا بالزمان فيجوز مثله هاهنا بأن يكون الإيجاد القصدي مع وجود المقصود زماناً ومتقدماً عليه بالذات. ولا فرق بينهما أي بين الإيجادين فيما يعود إلى السبق واقتضاء العدم. وحينئذ جاز أن يكون العالم واجباً في الأزل بالواجب لذاته تعالى مع كونه مختاراً، فيكونان معاً في الوجود وإن تفاوتا في التقدم والتأخر بحسب الذات، كما أن حركة اليد سابقة على حركة الخاتم بالذات، وإن كانت معها في الزمان.
The caused thing is like the priority of creation by necessary emanation. For just as that, i.e., the priority of the creation of the necessary (al-ījād al-ījābī), is a priority in essence (bil-dhāt) not in time (bil-zamān), so likewise it is permissible here that voluntary creation (al-ījād al-qasdī) be simultaneous with the existence of the intended object in time while being prior to it in essence. And there is no difference between them, i.e., between the two creations, regarding what pertains to priority and requiring non-existence. Then it is permissible that the world be necessary in pre-eternity (al-azal) by the Necessary by virtue of His Essence, exalted is He, despite His being voluntary (mukhtār); thus they both coexist in existence, even though they differ in priority and posteriority with respect to essence, just as the movement of the hand is prior to the movement of the ring in essence, even though it is simultaneous with it in time.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of divine creation: necessary emanation (ijābī) and voluntary creation (qasdī). The key point is that the priority of the cause over the effect can be in essence (bil-dhāt) not in time. The author argues that voluntary creation can also be simultaneous with its effect in time while being prior in essence, just as the hand movement is prior to the ring movement. This allows the world to be eternal (qadīm) while being created by a voluntary agent. The passage establishes the fundamental distinction between temporal and essential priority.

Key Terms
سبق بالذات
سبق بالزمان
إيجاد إيجابي
إيجاد قصدي
واجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that priority in time is necessary for causation.
Correction: This passage shows that essential priority suffices; cause and effect can be simultaneous in time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of priority does voluntary creation (الإيجاد القصدي) have over the intended object?
A) Temporal priority (سبق بالزمان)
B) Essential priority (سبق بالذات)
C) Both temporal and essential
D) No priority

Key Takeaway
Divine causation requires essential priority, not temporal priority, allowing the world to be eternal yet created by a voluntary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face circumstances that seem delayed or out of alignment with your expectations, do you remember that God's causation is essential, not temporal? How might this reshape your patience and trust in His wisdom?
?
The hand-and-ring analogy shows that cause and effect can coexist in time while differing in essence. In your relationship with God, are you looking for temporal priority as proof of His action, or can you see the essential priority of His will in the present moment?
?
If the world is necessary by God yet still voluntary, that means every moment of existence is both a necessary consequence of His essence and a free gift of His will. Does this inspire you to live with deeper gratitude and responsibility? Why or why not?

Critique of the Philosophers' Claim of Divine Free Will
Refutation of philosophers' free will claim via conditional analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' claim that God is a free agent is inconsistent with their own doctrine of necessary emanation.

Core Idea
True free will requires real alternatives; philosophers' necessity contradicts it.

In this passage, the author first presents the philosophers' argument that God is a free agent based on conditional statements. Then he refutes it, showing that their own system of necessary emanation (ījāb) undermines genuine free will. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and conditional
Read the first part where the philosophers claim God is a free agent, defined by conditional statements: if He wills He acts, if He wills He refrains. Notice that the truth of the conditional does not require the actual occurrence of the antecedent.
The philosophers use a logical distinction to defend free will.
2
Understand the refutation's structure
The refutation from al-Dhakhīra argues that the philosophers' own position of necessary emanation contradicts this claim. It presents two possible interpretations of the conditional's perpetuity.
Focus on the two 'if they intend' clauses.
3
Analyze the first interpretation
If they mean the antecedents are perpetual while their opposites are possible, this contradicts their explicit statement that God is necessary by essence for the world, making non-occurrence impossible.
4
Analyze the second interpretation
If they mean the antecedents are perpetual while their opposites are impossible, then there is no real will or choice—only empty words.
This shows the inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The critics argue that genuine free will requires the possibility of both action and refrainment. The philosophers' system cannot accommodate this, so their claim is unverified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤيد كلام الآمدي ما نقله بعضهم من أن الحكماء متفقون على أنه تعالى فاعل مختار بمعنى إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، وصدق الشرطية لا يقتضي وقوع مقدمها ولا عدم وقوعه، فمقدم شرطية الفعل واقع دائماً، ومقدم شرطية الترك غير واقع دائماً. قوله : (من أن الحكماء متفقون على أنه تعالى فاعل مختار) قال الأستاذ المحقق في الذخيرة، هذا المنقول عنهم كلام لا تحقيق له لأن الواقع بالإرادة والاختيار ما يصح وجوده وعدمه بالنظر إلى ذات الفاعل، فإن أريد بدوام وقوع مقدم الشرطية الأولى وعدم وقوع الثانية دوامهما مع صحة وقوع نقيضهما فهذا مخالف لما هم مصرحون به من كونه تعالى موجباً بالذات للعالم، بحيث لا يصح عدم وقوعه منه. وإن أريد دوامهما مع امتناع نقيضهما فليس هناك حقيقة الإرادة والاختيار بل مجرد اللفظ.
And what supports the statement of al-Āmidī is what some have narrated that the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') agree that He, exalted, is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār) in the sense that if He wills He acts and if He wills He refrains, and the truth of the conditional does not require the occurrence of its antecedent nor its non-occurrence; thus the antecedent of the conditional of action always occurs, and the antecedent of the conditional of refrainment never occurs. Statement: (that the philosophers agree that He, exalted, is a free agent) The verifying teacher said in al-Dhakhīra: This reported statement of theirs is an unverified statement, because what is by will and choice (al-irāda wal-ikhtiyār) is that whose existence and non-existence are both possible with respect to the agent's essence. If they intend by the perpetual occurrence of the antecedent of the first conditional and perpetual non-occurrence of the second that they are perpetual while their opposites are possible, then this contradicts what they explicitly state, namely that He, exalted, is necessary by essence (mūjib bi-l-dhāt) for the world, such that its non-occurrence from Him is impossible. And if they intend that they are perpetual while their opposites are impossible, then there is no reality to will and choice, only mere wording.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that the philosophers (ḥukamā') agree that God is a free agent. It first quotes al-Āmidī's support for that claim based on the conditional statements. Then it quotes a refutation from 'al-Ustādh al-Muḥaqqiq' in al-Dhakhīra, arguing that the philosophers' own position of divine necessity (ījāb) contradicts genuine free will. True free will requires that both existence and non-existence be possible. The philosophers hold that the world necessarily emanates from God, so they cannot consistently claim that God is free in the sense of having real alternatives.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
شرطية
موجب بالذات
إرادة
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' claim that God is a free agent can be accepted at face value.
Correction: The philosophers' doctrine of necessary emanation (ījāb) makes genuine free will impossible, as true choice requires the possibility of alternatives.

Checkpoint
What is the critique's central argument against the philosophers' claim that God is a free agent?
A) They deny free will entirely
B) Their system of necessary emanation contradicts genuine freedom
C) They misinterpret the conditional
D) They agree with the mutakallimun

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim of divine free will is inconsistent with their own doctrine of necessary emanation, making it either contradictory or merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim that God has free will, do you truly believe that He could have done differently? If not, have you reduced God's will to a mechanical necessity without realizing it?
?
The philosophers used the language of free will while holding a system that denied real alternatives. Where in your own life do you use religious words like 'trust' or 'submission' but actually live as though outcomes are fixed and your choices don't matter?
?
This passage insists that true will requires the possibility of the opposite. Do your prayers reflect a belief that God genuinely hears and can respond differently, or have you fallen into a fatalism that makes your worship hollow?

First Objection: Non-existence Cannot Be a Condition
Presents the first objection: non-existence is incompatible with existence and agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection argues that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for an effect's existence and agency, and how the rebuttal addresses a counterargument about time.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be a condition for existence because it is incompatible with the very thing it conditions.

This lesson unpacks the first objection raised against the Imam's argument that denies causal dependency of eternal beings. The objection targets the notion that prior non-existence could serve as a condition for an effect's existence. Using logical incompatibility, it argues that if non-existence were a condition, it would contradict the nature of existence and agency. We will also see a brief rebuttal concerning the second moment of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is structured.
The passage has two parts: the objection and a rebuttal about the second time.
2
Identify the core claim
The objection claims that non-existence is incompatible with existence and agency. Find the statement in the passage.
Look for the phrase 'العدم ينافي الوجود والفاعلية'.
3
Trace the reasoning
The objection argues: since incompatibility exists, prior non-existence cannot be a condition. Follow the logical steps from incompatibility to non-conditionality.
The key is that a condition cannot be incompatible with what it conditions.
4
Understand the rebuttal
The passage ends with a rebuttal about the second moment of time. It says the non-existence in the second moment is not a real existent but a relational matter.
This addresses a potential counterargument that non-existence could be an additional thing.
5
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider why this objection matters: if prior non-existence is not a condition, then an effect can be eternal yet caused, challenging the Imam's denial of causal dependency for the eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاول : العدم ينافي الوجود والفاعلية ) أي عدم الأثر ينافي وجوده وهذا ظاهر وينافي أيضاً فاعلية الفاعل لذلك الأثر؛ لأن تلك الفاعلية ملزومة لذلك الوجود ومنافي اللازم مناف للملزوم . وإذا كان كذلك ( فلا يكون السابق منه أي من عدم الأثر ) شرطاً لهما ) أي لوجود الأثر وكون الفاعل فاعلاً له ضرورة أن شرط الشيء لا ينافيه . وإذا لم يكن العدم السابق شرطاً لهما جاز أن يكون الأثر المستند إلى الفاعل غير مسبوق بالعدم، وهو المطلوب. الزمان الثاني فلا يرد ما قيل من أنه لا يلزم من كونه زائداً كونه موجوداً لجواز أن يكون أمراً اعتبارياً متجدداً.
The first: Non-existence is incompatible with existence and effective agency) i.e., the non-existence of the effect is incompatible with its existence, and this is apparent, and it is also incompatible with the effective agency of the agent for that effect; because that effective agency is entailed by that existence, and what is incompatible with the entailed is incompatible with the entailer. And since this is the case, (the prior [non-existence] i.e., the non-existence of the effect) is not a condition for them) i.e., for the existence of the effect and for the agent being an agent for it, necessarily because the condition of a thing is not incompatible with it. And if the prior non-existence is not a condition for them, then it is permissible for the effect dependent on the agent to be unpreceded by non-existence, which is the desired. The second time: then it is not objected what is said that it does not necessitate from its being additional that it is existent, because it is permissible for it to be a relational matter that is renewed.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the Imam's argument that denies the causal dependency of the eternal. The objection argues that non-existence (ʿadam) is incompatible with existence (wujūd) and effective agency (fāʿiliyya). Since the condition of a thing cannot be incompatible with it, the prior non-existence of an effect cannot be a condition for its existence or for the agent being an agent. Therefore, an effect can be unpreceded by non-existence, meaning it can be eternal yet caused. The final sentence addresses a potential counterargument about time, stating that the non-existence in the second moment is not a real existent but a relational matter.

Key Terms
عدم
وجود
فاعلية
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can be a condition because it precedes existence.
Correction: The objection shows that incompatibility prevents conditionality; a condition cannot be incompatible with what it conditions.

Checkpoint
Why does the first objection claim that prior non-existence cannot be a condition for existence?
A) Because non-existence is a temporal thing
B) Because non-existence is incompatible with existence and agency
C) Because the condition must be simultaneous with the conditioned
D) Because non-existence is a relational matter

Key Takeaway
Prior non-existence cannot be a condition for existence because it contradicts the nature of existence and agency, allowing for an eternal yet caused effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay an act of obedience because you feel you are not yet 'ready'—does that treating non-existence as a condition conflict with this lesson's principle? What would change if you acted despite the lack?
?
Consider a area of your life where you are waiting for circumstances to be perfect before you commit to God. How does the argument that non-existence cannot be a condition challenge your delay?
?
If God's agency does not require a prior state of non-existence for His effects, what does that imply about your own need to 'prepare' yourself before seeking His mercy? How can you act on this today?

Divine Omniscience Is Identical to Knowledge
Commentator declares omniscience identical to knowledge and refutes states theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine omniscience is identical to knowledge itself, not a separate state, and how this refutes the Ash'arite theory of states (ahwal).

Core Idea
God's attribute of knowing is His knowledge itself, not a distinct entity.

This passage introduces a key Maturidi doctrine: the attribute of being all-knowing (العالمية) is not a separate state that causes knowledge; it is identical to knowledge. This directly contradicts the Ash'arite theory of states. Let's read the text and see how the commentator argues against that theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once in Arabic and then its translation. Notice the strong assertion at the start.
2
Identify the identity claim
The commentator states 'والعالمية عندنا نفس العلم' – omniscience is identical to knowledge. Recognize this as a direct denial of the states theory.
3
Understand the refutation of states
The phrase 'العلم لا معللة به مع قدمهما' means knowledge is not caused by the attribute, given both are eternal. The commentator targets those who accept states (الحال).
This refutes a specific Ash'arite position.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why distinguishing attribute from essence leads to the states problem. This passage clarifies that attributes like 'knowing' are not separate realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعالمية عندنا نفس العلم وإرادتنا غير مؤثرة. العلم لا معللة به مع قدمهما كما ادعيتموه، نعم يتجه هذا على القائل بالحال . وإرادتنا غير مؤثرة أي لا مدخل لها في وجود أفعالنا ، فلذلك جاز تعلقها بالموجود الباقي حال بقائه إذ لا تأثير منا هناك ابتداءً ولا دواماً فلا محذور، بخلاف ما إذا تعلق به التأثير إرادياً كان أو إيجابياً فإنه يستلزم إيجاد الموجود.
And omniscience with us is identical to knowledge, and our will is not effective. Knowledge is not caused by it with the eternity of both as you have claimed; yes, this is directed at the one who posits states. And our will is not effective, meaning it has no role in the existence of our actions, and therefore it is permissible for it to be attached to the existent that persists while it remains persistent, since we have no influence there initially nor permanently, so there is no problem, unlike when influence is attached to it, whether voluntary or necessitating, for then it would entail creating the already existent.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage explains two doctrines. First, 'العالمية' (the attribute of being knowing) is identical to knowledge itself, not a separate state ('حال') causing knowledge. This refutes the Ash'arite theory of states (ahwal). The commentator adds that this claim—that knowledge is not caused by the attribute—applies against those who posit states, not against others. Second, 'our will is not effective' means human will has no causal role in producing our actions. Therefore, it is permissible for the will to be directed at an already existing thing while it remains persistent, because we do not cause its existence at any time. If our will did cause existence, directing it at an already existent thing would entail recreating what already exists, which is absurd. This distinction supports the argument that the eternal can depend on a necessary agent without implying temporal origination.

Key Terms
العالمية
العلم
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attribute of 'knowing' is a distinct state that causes His knowledge, separate from His essence.
Correction: The text clarifies that omniscience is identical to knowledge itself; there is no separate 'state' that causes knowledge. The attribute and knowledge are one and the same.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the commentator say 'نعم يتجه هذا على القائل بالحال'?
A) To agree that the argument applies only to those who deny states.
B) To affirm that the argument about knowledge not being caused by the attribute targets those who posit states.
C) To state that omniscience is a separate state from knowledge.
D) To indicate that human will is effective.

Key Takeaway
In Maturidi theology, the attribute of omniscience is identical to knowledge itself, undermining the Ash'arite theory of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since God's knowledge is identical to His attribute of knowing, there is no gap between what He knows and what He is. How does this reality affect the way you think about His awareness of your secret thoughts and deeds? Is there any part of your life you are trying to hide from His knowledge? Write down a commitment to be more conscious of His immediate presence.
?
The text says knowledge is not caused by the attribute. If God's knowledge is eternal and uncaused, what does that imply about the permanence of His judgment regarding your actions? In what way does this eternal knowledge give you certainty—or fear—about His decree? Identify one area where you need to submit more fully to His knowledge.
?
The rejection of 'states' means there is no intermediate layer between God and His knowledge. How does this nearness affect your confidence in His responses to your duas? Are you living as if His knowledge is immediate and personal, or distant and abstract? Name one change you will make today to live in light of His immediate omniscience.

intermediate
5 min
Human Will Is Not Causally Effective
Commentator explains human will's non-causality and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that human will has no causal role in producing actions, and why it is permissible for the will to be attached to an already existing thing.

Core Idea
Human will does not cause actions; it merely accompanies them.

Here the commentator explains the second doctrine: our will has no causal role in producing our actions. This seemingly paradoxical statement has important implications for the object of the will. Let's see why it is acceptable to will an already existing thing without absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the definition of 'not effective': لا مدخل لها في وجود أفعالنا.
2
Understand the meaning of 'not effective'
The commentator explains that our will has no role in the existence of our actions. It does not cause them; rather, it is a property that accompanies them.
This is a key point in Maturidi occasionalism.
3
Trace the implication for willing persistent things
Because the will is not effective, it can be attached to an already existing thing (e.g., willing the continuation of a created being) without implying that we are creating it anew.
The absence of causal influence means no risk of 'creating the already existent'.
4
Contrast with effective will
The passage ends by noting that if the will were effective (either voluntary or necessitating), then attaching it to an existent would entail creating what already exists (إيجاد الموجود), which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعالمية عندنا نفس العلم وإرادتنا غير مؤثرة. العلم لا معللة به مع قدمهما كما ادعيتموه، نعم يتجه هذا على القائل بالحال . وإرادتنا غير مؤثرة أي لا مدخل لها في وجود أفعالنا ، فلذلك جاز تعلقها بالموجود الباقي حال بقائه إذ لا تأثير منا هناك ابتداءً ولا دواماً فلا محذور، بخلاف ما إذا تعلق به التأثير إرادياً كان أو إيجابياً فإنه يستلزم إيجاد الموجود.
And omniscience with us is identical to knowledge, and our will is not effective. Knowledge is not caused by it with the eternity of both as you have claimed; yes, this is directed at the one who posits states. And our will is not effective, meaning it has no role in the existence of our actions, and therefore it is permissible for it to be attached to the existent that persists while it remains persistent, since we have no influence there initially nor permanently, so there is no problem, unlike when influence is attached to it, whether voluntary or necessitating, for then it would entail creating the already existent.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage explains two doctrines. First, 'العالمية' (the attribute of being knowing) is identical to knowledge itself, not a separate state ('حال') causing knowledge. This refutes the Ash'arite theory of states (ahwal). The commentator adds that this claim—that knowledge is not caused by the attribute—applies against those who posit states, not against others. Second, 'our will is not effective' means human will has no causal role in producing our actions. Therefore, it is permissible for the will to be directed at an already existing thing while it remains persistent, because we do not cause its existence at any time. If our will did cause existence, directing it at an already existent thing would entail recreating what already exists, which is absurd. This distinction supports the argument that the eternal can depend on a necessary agent without implying temporal origination.

Key Terms
الإرادة
إيجاد الموجود
تعلق الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that human will has the power to bring about actions, i.e., it is causally effective.
Correction: The text teaches that human will has no causal role in the existence of actions; actions are created by God alone. The will merely accompanies them.

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for the human will to be attached to an already existing thing, according to the passage?
A) Because the will is ineffective, so it does not necessitate creating anew.
B) Because the will is creative and can bring things into existence.
C) Because the will of God is never attached to eternal things.
D) Because human actions are caused by God, not by human will.

Key Takeaway
Human will is not effective; thus it can be directed at existing things without absurdity, preserving God's exclusive creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan or desire something, do you feel that your will is the driving force that makes it happen? How does the truth that your will has no causal effect challenge your sense of control and dependence on Allah alone?
?
This lesson teaches that even your will to continue existing does not cause your life to persist. What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you truly internalized that you have no part in sustaining your own existence?

The Eternity of God's Essence and Attributes
Author discusses agreement on God's essence eternity and Ash'ari view on attributes, with opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological consensus on the eternity of God's essence and the differing views on whether His attributes are also eternal, focusing on the Ash'ari position and its opponents.

Core Idea
God's essence is eternal by universal agreement; His attributes are eternal according to the Ash'aris, but others disagree.

This passage from al-Mawaqif addresses the second matter concerning the eternal (al-qadim). It first establishes that all philosophers and people of religion agree that God's essence is described by eternity. Then it presents the Ash'ari view that God's attributes are also eternal, existent, and subsisting in His essence, contrasting with the Mu'tazilites, philosophers, the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), and the Karramiyya. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the Islamic discourse on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation to get an overview of the discussion.
Notice how the author uses the phrase 'agreement of the philosophers and the people of religion'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins by stating that describing God's essence with qadim (eternity) is agreed upon by all. This is the first key point.
3
Recognize the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris extend eternity also to God's attributes, saying they are existent, eternal, and subsisting in His essence.
This is the central doctrine being defended.
4
Understand the opposing views
The commentary details three oppositions: a) philosophers and Mu'tazilites deny attributes altogether; b) verifiers among theologians and Sufis consider attributes as mental relations; c) Karramiyya hold attributes are originated.
Each group rejects the Ash'ari view in a different way.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider why these distinctions matter for understanding God's nature and how they shape different theological schools.
This touches on the broader debate about God's attributes in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وثانيهما ) أي ثاني الأمرين من مباحث القديم ( أنه يوصف به أي بالقدم ( ذات الله تعالى (اتفاقا من الحكماء وأهل الملة (و) يوصف به أيضاً ( صفاته عند الأشاعرة ) ومن يحذو حذوهم فإنهم أجمعوا على أن لله سبحانه صفات موجودة قديمة قائمة بذاته تعالى ( وأما المعتزلة فأنكروه (لفظاً ) أي أنكروا أن يوصف. قوله : ( إن لله سبحانه وتعالى صفات) خلافاً للحكماء والمعتزلة حيث نفوا الصفات وأثبتوا الثمرات . قوله: (موجودة) خلافاً للمحققين من المتكلمين والصوفية حيث قالوا: إن علمه عبارة عن التعلق المخصوص بين العالم والمعلوم وقدرته عن التمكن وإرادته عن تخصيص أحد المقدورين وكذا السمع والبصر فهي أمور اعتبارية زائدة على ذاته يترتب عليها ثمراتها . قوله : ( قديمة) خلافاً للكرامية القائلين بحدوثها وتجويز كون ذاته تعالى محلاً للحوادث.
And the second of the two matters from the investigations of the eternal (qadim): that it is described by it, i.e., by eternity, (the essence of God, exalted) (by agreement of the philosophers and the people of the religion), and it is also described by it (His attributes according to the Ash'aris) and those who follow their path, for they have agreed that God, glorified, has attributes that are existent, eternal, subsisting in His essence, exalted. But the Mu'tazilites denied it (verbally), i.e., they denied that it is described. His saying: (Indeed God, glorified and exalted, has attributes) contrary to the philosophers and Mu'tazilites, for they negated attributes and affirmed the results. His saying: (existent) contrary to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) among the theologians and Sufis, for they said that His knowledge is the specific relation between the knower and the known, His power is ability, His will is specification of one of the two determined things, likewise hearing and sight; they are mental attributes additional to His essence, upon which their results depend. His saying: (eternal) contrary to the Karramiyya, who said they are originated and deemed permissible that His essence, exalted, be a locus for originated things.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the second matter concerning investigations of the eternal (qadim): that God's essence is described by eternity by agreement of philosophers and people of religion, and His attributes are also described by eternity according to the Ash'aris and those who follow them. The Mu'tazilites verbally denied describing attributes as eternal. The commentary then contrasts the views: the philosophers and Mu'tazilites deny attributes altogether (affirming only results), the verifiers among theologians and Sufis consider attributes as mental relations added to the essence, and the Karramiyya hold that attributes are originated and that God's essence can be a locus for originated things. The commentary explains each qualification of the attributes (existent, eternal) against opposing groups.

Key Terms
القدم
الصفات
الأشاعرة
المعتزلة
الحكماء
الكرامية
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims agree that God's attributes are eternal.
Correction: Only the Ash'aris and their followers affirm that attributes are eternal; other groups like the Mu'tazilites, philosophers, verifiers, and Karramiyya hold different views.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group agrees that God's attributes are existent, eternal, and subsisting in His essence?
A) The Mu'tazilites
B) The Karramiyya
C) The Ash'aris
D) The philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm that both God's essence and attributes are eternal, while other groups deny or reinterpret the eternity of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, power, and will, do you truly consider them as real and eternal as He is, or do you sometimes treat them as abstract concepts? How might your trust in God deepen if you vividly remember that His attributes never change or fade? This week, choose one attribute—like His hearing—and intentionally speak to Him as the One who hears you at this very moment, knowing that His hearing is eternal and personal.
?
The passage shows that some groups reduce God's attributes to mere mental relations or deny them outright. Have you ever, in moments of doubt or difficulty, unconsciously stripped God of His personal attributes, making Him distant or impersonal? What practical step can you take today to reconnect with the reality that God's mercy, justice, and power are not just ideas but active and eternal features of His being that affect your life right now?
?
If the Ash'ari view is correct—that God's attributes are distinct and eternal—then every attribute is a direct channel to knowing Him more intimately. Are you content with a vague theology, or will you deliberately study one attribute each week to let it transform your worship and daily decisions? Commit to spending ten minutes before your next prayer reflecting on one eternal attribute and its implications for your current struggles.

Mu'tazilite Affirmation of States (Aḥwāl)
Mu'tazilites affirm four states and Abu Hashim adds a fifth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites affirmed eternal states for God while verbally denying eternal attributes, and the addition of a fifth state by Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites replaced eternal attributes with eternal states to avoid multiple eternals.

The Mu'tazilites attempted to uphold divine unity by denying that God's attributes are eternal beings distinct from His essence. However, they still needed to account for God's attributes such as life, knowledge, and power. Their solution was the theory of 'states' (aḥwāl) — realities that are not existent but are 'established' eternally. In this lesson, we explore the four states they affirmed and the fifth state added by Abu Hashim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning. Then read the translation.
Note that the Mu'tazilites are described as denying eternal attributes verbally but affirming them in meaning.
2
Identify the four states
The passage lists four states: existence, life, knowledge, and power. They are interpreted as 'being existent', 'being living', etc.
These are considered 'established' (thābita) for God eternally.
3
Understand the fifth state
Abu Hashim adds a fifth state: divinity (ilāhiyya), which is the cause of the other four and distinguishes God's essence.
This fifth state is unique to Abu Hashim's school within Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالقدم ما سوى الله تعالى، سواء كان صفة له أو لم يكن إنكاراً بحسب اللفظ . ( لكن قالوا به معنى فإنهم أثبتوا (له أي لله تعالى ( أحوالاً أربعة لا أول لها : هي الوجود والحياة والعلم والقدرة ) أي الموجودية والحيية والعالمية ،والقادرية، فإنها أحوال ثابتة لله سبحانه وتعالى أزلاً (و) أثبت (أبو هاشم) منهم حالة ( خامسة) هي (علة للأربعة ) المذكورة ( ومميزة للذات ) أي لذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات المساوية له في الذاتية (هي الإلهية). فقد أثبتوا مع الله في الأزل أموراً كثيرة فلزمهم تعدد القديم مع تحاشيهم عن إطلاق القديم على غير الله . ( كذا قال الإمام الرازي، وفيه نظر لأن القديم موجود لا أول له وهذه الأمور التي أثبتوها ( أحوال ) لا توصف عندهم بالوجود، فلا تكون قديمة إلا أن يراد بالقديم ثابت لا أول له، لكن الكلام في المعنى المشهور. وأيضاً إنما يلزم هذا من أثبت منهم الحال دون من عداهم.
قوله : (أي أنكروا إلخ) يعني أن الضمير راجع إلى ما يفهم من كون صفاته تعالى قديمة وهو كون ما سوى ذاته قديماً وليس راجعاً إلى المذكور لأنه يشعر بأنهم قالوا بالصفات لكنهم أنكروا قدمها .
قوله : (أي الموجودية إلخ) فسرها بتلك لأنها من الصفات الموجودة لا الأحوال .
قوله : ( هي الإلهية ) أي الواجبة .
قوله : ( لا توصف عندهم بالوجود إلخ) بل بالتحقق الذي يرادف الثبوت الشامل للموجود والحال والمعدوم الممكن وما قيل في دفع النظر لا معنى للوجود إلا ما عنوا بالثبوت فلا فرق في المعنى بين قولنا لا أول لوجوده ولا أول لثبوته حتى لو نوقش في اللفظ غيرنا الوجود إلى الثبوت ليس بشيء.
By 'eternal' is meant everything other than God, whether it is an attribute of Him or not, as a denial in terms of wording. But they affirmed it in meaning, for they affirmed for Him (i.e., for God) four states that have no beginning: existence, life, knowledge, and power — i.e., being existent, being living, being knowing, and being powerful. They are states established for God, exalted, eternally. And Abu Hashim among them affirmed a fifth state, which is the cause of the four and distinguisher of the essence — i.e., His essence from other essences equal in essentia — which is divinity. So they affirmed with God in eternity many things, and thus multiple eternals necessarily followed for them, despite their avoidance of applying 'eternal' to other than God. Thus said Imam al-Razi. But there is consideration: because the eternal is an existent that has no beginning, and these things they affirmed are states, which according to them are not described by existence, so they are not eternal unless by 'eternal' is meant 'established with no beginning'. But the discussion is about the famous meaning. Also, this only follows from those among them who affirm the state, not from others.
His statement: (i.e., they denied etc.) means the pronoun refers to that which is understood from the fact that His attributes are eternal, which is that what is other than His essence is eternal, not to the mentioned, because it implies that they affirmed the attributes but denied their eternity.
His statement: (i.e., being existent etc.) He interpreted them with those because they are from the existent attributes, not states.
His statement: (it is divinity) i.e., the necessary.
His statement: (they are not described by existence according to them etc.) Rather, by 'taḥaqquq' which is synonymous with 'thubūt' encompassing the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent. And what was said in rebutting the consideration — that there is no meaning of existence except what they meant by thubūt, so there is no difference in meaning between 'no beginning to its existence' and 'no beginning to its thubūt', such that if one objected to the wording, he would change existence to thubūt — is nothing.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a definition and critique of the Mu'tazilite position on divine attributes. It first explains that the Mu'tazilites, while verbally denying that anything other than God is eternal, actually affirmed eternal states (aḥwāl) — four states (existence, life, knowledge, power) plus a fifth (divinity) according to Abu Hashim. The commentator clarifies that these states are not described as 'existent' in their terminology, but are 'established'. Al-Razi then criticizes that if these states have no beginning, they are effectively 'eternal' according to the usual meaning, so the Mu'tazilites are guilty of positing multiple eternals. The commentator notes that this criticism applies only to those Mu'tazilites who affirm states. The commentary explains each phrase: the pronoun in 'they denied' refers to the implication that the attributes are eternal, and the interpretation of 'al-mawjūdiyya' etc. as existent attributes rather than states. The final comment defends al-Razi's criticism by arguing that there is no meaningful difference between existence and thubūt in this context, so the objection that states are not existent is irrelevant.

Key Terms
قديم
حال
أحوال
الوجود
الحياة
العلم
القدرة
الموجودية
الحيية
العالمية
القادرية
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many states did the Mu'tazilites as a whole affirm?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Three

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites affirmed eternal states for God to account for His attributes without calling them eternal entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Knowing' or 'Powerful', do you ever feel a tension between His oneness and the multiplicity of attributes? How does the Mu'tazilite attempt to resolve this with 'states' challenge you to refine your own understanding of divine unity?
?
The Mu'tazilites risked theological censure for their states theory. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that, if scrutinized, might be closer to what you criticize in others? What are you uncritically accepting without examining its implications for tawḥīd?
?
If states are real distinctions in God's essence, how does that affect your worship? Are you relating to a God with distinct parts, or to a simple essence with multiple descriptions? How does this lesson push you to be more precise in your devotional language?

Al-Razi's Critique of Mu'tazilite States
Al-Razi criticizes states as implying multiple eternals; commentator responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the Mu'tazilite states are effectively multiple eternals and the commentator's defense of al-Razi.

Core Idea
Al-Razi argues that states with no beginning are eternal in meaning, so multiple eternals are unavoidable.

Al-Razi, a leading Ash'ari theologian, identified a fatal inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite theory of states. If states are 'established' with no beginning, then they are, in the commonly understood sense, eternal. This leads to the very multiplicity the Mu'tazilites sought to avoid. In this lesson, we examine al-Razi's criticism and the commentator's subsequent defense.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's criticism
Al-Razi says the Mu'tazilites affirmed many things in eternity, so multiple eternals necessarily follow.
2
Understand the objection
The commentator notes that states are not existent, so they cannot be called eternal in the usual sense.
This limits the criticism to those who accept states.
3
See the rebuttal
The commentator ultimately argues that thubut (establishment) is synonymous with existence, so the criticism holds.
There is no difference between 'no beginning to its existence' and 'no beginning to its thubut'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( لكن قالوا به معنى فإنهم أثبتوا (له أي لله تعالى ( أحوالاً أربعة لا أول لها : هي الوجود والحياة والعلم والقدرة ) أي الموجودية والحيية والعالمية ،والقادرية، فإنها أحوال ثابتة لله سبحانه وتعالى أزلاً (و) أثبت (أبو هاشم) منهم حالة ( خامسة) هي (علة للأربعة ) المذكورة ( ومميزة للذات ) أي لذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات المساوية له في الذاتية (هي الإلهية). فقد أثبتوا مع الله في الأزل أموراً كثيرة فلزمهم تعدد القديم مع تحاشيهم عن إطلاق القديم على غير الله . The commentator notes that this criticism applies only to those Mu'tazilites who affirm states. The commentary explains each phrase: the pronoun in 'they denied' refers to the implication that the attributes are eternal, and the interpretation of 'al-mawjūdiyya' etc. as existent attributes rather than states. The final comment defends al-Razi's criticism by arguing that there is no meaningful difference between existence and thubūt in this context, so the objection that states are not existent is irrelevant.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's criticism applies to all Mu'tazilites equally.
Correction: The passage states that the criticism only applies to those who affirm the states, not to those who deny them.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does al-Razi's criticism still hold despite the Mu'tazilites' claim that states are not existent?
A) Because the Mu'tazilites explicitly call the states eternal.
B) Because the states have no beginning, and thubut is synonymous with existence in meaning.
C) Because the commentator disagrees with al-Razi.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's criticism exposes a fundamental inconsistency: states with no beginning are effectively eternal.

?
Al-Razi's critique forces us to examine our words carefully. In what areas of your faith might you be using terms loosely that conceal deeper inconsistencies? What step will you take this week to clarify a theological concept you've been confused about?
?
The commentator defends al-Razi by showing that thubut and existence are synonymous. What intellectual assumptions do you hold that you have never questioned? Are you willing to examine them as rigorously as al-Razi examined the Mu'tazilites?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Mu'tazilite Argument against Eternal Attributes and the Ash'ari Response
Mu'tazilite proof and Ash'ari response distinguishing attributes from essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that affirming multiple eternal attributes amounts to disbelief, and the Ash'ari response distinguishing between attributes and essences.

Core Idea
Multiple eternals are unbelief — unless they are attributes, not independent essences.

The Mu'tazilites challenge the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal attributes by comparing it to Christian Trinitarianism, which is unanimously considered disbelief. The Ash'ari response draws a crucial distinction: Christians affirmed the hypostases as separate essences, whereas Ash'aris affirm attributes inherent in one essence. This passage presents that exchange and the Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite proof
Identify the claim: saying there are multiple eternals is kufr by consensus; Christians were made kafir for affirming three eternal descriptions with God's essence.
The Mu'tazilites equate Ash'ari attributes with Christian hypostases.
2
Examine the Ash'ari response
Notice the response: Christians were disbelieved because they affirmed the hypostases as essences, not attributes. They believed in the transfer of the hypostasis of knowledge to Jesus, which implies substantial independence.
3
Consider the Quranic evidence
The Ash'ari cites the Quran: 'They have disbelieved who say Allah is the third of three' — the condemnation is for affirming three gods, not three attributes.
This shows the rejection is of multiple divine essences, not multiple attributes in one God.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Understand why Ash'ari attributes are not like Christian hypostases: attributes are not independent selves.
The key is the concept of transfer, which only applies to independent beings.

The Mu'tazilites argued for the denial of the eternal attributes that the Ash'aris affirmed: because the statement of multiple eternals is unbelief by consensus, and the Christians were only disbelieved because they affirmed with His essence attributes — i.e., descriptions.
His statement: (i.e., descriptions) He interpreted 'attributes' as 'descriptions' to justify his saying 'three' although the apparent is 'three'.
Three eternal things they called 'hypostases', which are in the meaning of principles; its singular is 'hypostasis'. Al-Jawhari said: I think it is Greek. They are knowledge, existence, and life. They expressed existence as the Father, life as the Holy Spirit, and knowledge as the Word. In some copies, 'power' occurs instead of 'existence' and it is a mistake. So how can one not be disbelieved who affirms with His essence seven of the famous eternal descriptions or more, such as when adding to them takwīn (origination) or other existential attributes about which there is disagreement like subsisting, hand, etc.? And the answer: The Christians were only disbelieved because they affirmed them — i.e., the mentioned hypostases — as essences, not attributes, even though they avoided calling them essences and called them attributes; for they said that the hypostasis of knowledge (which is the Word) transferred to the Messiah, and what is independent in transfer can only be an essence. And affirming multiple eternal essences is unbelief by consensus, unlike affirming eternal attributes in one essence. Also, God only disbelieved them by His saying: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' [5:73], for their affirmation of three gods, as indicated by His saying after it: 'And there is no god except one God' [5:73]. His statement: (they called them hypostases because they are principles of creation; perhaps they refer power and will back to knowledge.)
His statement: (and that which is independent in transfer) This only holds on their saying of actual transfer; but if by it is meant complete manifestation and self-disclosure, it does not hold. Also, committing unbelief is unbelief, not its implication. And what was said — that the necessity of essentiality for real transfer is clear and is like commitment — is rejected, as some have held the permissibility of transfer upon accidents, and that its being like commitment does not necessitate takfir because of the presence of doubt.
His statement: (unlike affirming eternal attributes etc.) Because it does not necessitate making Him necessitated, since the necessitating agent and the choosing agent are two types of agent, and His essence is not an agent for His attributes, else He would be prior to them in existence, rather He necessitates them.
His statement: (as indicated by etc.) Means that what is intended is 'third of three' in divinity, i.e., deserving of worship, as evidenced by His saying: 'And there is no god except one God' [5:73].
His statement: (i.e., descriptions) He interpreted attributes as descriptions to justify his saying 'three' although the apparent is 'three'.

So it does not occur what was imagined — that unbelief is committing unbelief, not its implication. And it may be said: some Christians do not say transfer, rather connection or illumination, so the mainstay in the takfir of all of them is their affirmation of three gods and their denial of the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him.
His statement: (for their affirmation of three gods) Their takfir is not because they affirm necessity of existence for each of the three, since it has been stated in Ilāhiyyāt that no one differs on the question of tawḥīd of the necessary existent except the dualists, not
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazilite proof against eternal attributes and the Ash'ari response, focusing on the Christian example. The Mu'tazilites argue: saying there are multiple eternals is kufr by consensus; Christians were declared kafir because they affirmed three eternal attributes (hypostases) with God. Thus, the Ash'aris who affirm seven or more eternal attributes should also be kafir. The Ash'ari answer distinguishes: Christians affirmed these as essences (dhāt), not attributes. The commentator clarifies that 'attributes' here is interpreted as 'descriptions' to justify the phrase 'three'. The hypostases are identified as existence (Father), life (Holy Spirit), and knowledge (Word). The response emphasizes that Christians believed in the transfer of the hypostasis of knowledge to Jesus, which implies it is an essence, not an attribute. Attributes are inseparable from the essence, whereas transfer implies independence. Moreover, the Quranic verse condemns them for affirming three gods (third of three), not for affirming attributes. The commentary addresses nuances: the transfer argument works only for those Christians who believe in real transfer; for others, the primary reason for takfir is their belief in three gods and denial of Muhammad's prophethood. The passage also argues that affirming multiple eternal attributes in one essence does not entail making God 'necessitated' (like the Mu'tazilite view that God is 'mūjib' rather than 'mukhtār'), because God is not an agent for His attributes; rather, He necessitates them. The discussion concludes that the Christian case does not apply to Ash'ari attributes.

أقانيم
أقنوم

انتقال

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'ari belief in eternal attributes is identical to Christian Trinitarianism.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Christians affirm hypostases as separate essences, whereas Ash'aris affirm attributes as inherent descriptions of one essence, not independent entities.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, why were Christians declared disbelievers?
A) Because they affirmed three eternal attributes.
B) Because they affirmed the hypostases as essences and believed in transfer.
C) Because they denied the prophethood of Muhammad.
D) Because they used the term 'hypostasis'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument that eternal attributes imply kufr fails because Christians are guilty of affirming separate divine essences, not attributes within one essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending tawḥīd by rejecting eternal attributes, but they fell into a flawed analogy. Have you ever been so confident in a religious position that you misapplied an analogy and judged others unfairly? What would it take for you to re-examine that position today?
?
The Ash'ari response hinges on the distinction between attributes and essence. In your own understanding of Allah, do you sometimes think of His attributes—like His knowledge or power—as almost independent forces rather than perfections of His single, indivisible being? How can you deepen your awareness that His attributes are not separate from Him?
?
If it is true that affirming multiple eternal attributes within one essence is not kufr, then what does it mean for you that Allah alone has knowledge, power, and life that are eternal and inherent? How does that truth compel you to rely solely on Him and seek His attributes in your life right now?

Refining the Argument: Transfer and Divine Agency
Commentary on the transfer argument and the divine agency distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary on the transfer argument and the distinction between the necessitating agent (mūjib) and the choosing agent (mukhtār) regarding divine attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are not independent agents; they are necessitated by the divine essence.

This passage is a detailed commentary on the Ash'ari response. It first addresses the transfer argument: it only works if transfer is understood literally, not as manifestation. It also discusses whether implicating kufr is the same as committing kufr. Then it explains why affirming eternal attributes does not necessitate that God is mūjib rather than mukhtār, because God's essence is not an agent for His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the transfer argument
The commentator argues that the transfer argument only holds if Christians meant real transfer. If they meant manifestation, it fails.
This nuance shows the limits of the analogy.
2
Examine the issue of 'implication' vs 'commitment'
The passage discusses whether believing something that implies kufr makes one a kafir, noting that some allow doubt.
3
Learn the distinction between mūjib and mukhtār
The commentator explains that affirming eternal attributes does not make God necessitated, because His attributes are not separate agents; they are necessitated by His essence.
This is a key Ash'ari position against Mu'tazilite critiques.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you ever imagine them as independent forces rather than necessarily connected to His essence? How does this affect your worship and trust in His perfect unity?
?
The commentator notes that even a belief that implies unbelief does not automatically make a person a disbeliever due to doubt. Have you ever been quick to judge someone's faith based on a perceived implication rather than their actual belief? What steps can you take to avoid unjust takfir?
?
If God were compelled by His attributes, He would not be truly free. Reflect on an area of your life where you feel constrained by God's attributes—perhaps His justice seems harsh or His mercy seems limited. How can this lesson’s teaching on God’s free agency reshape your reliance on Him?
